The Second Meaning: Unconditional Possibility
Analysis of the second meaning of the statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation of the statement, where the act's occurrence in a time free from its non-existence is not impossible and does not entail any contradiction.

Core Idea
An act can exist in a time that is empty of its non-existence, which is intrinsically possible.

Having analyzed the first interpretation, the author presents a second, more nuanced meaning. Here, the act is considered in a time that is not characterized by its absence. This interpretation avoids the contradiction of the first and affirms the intrinsic possibility of the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the second meaning
Read the Arabic sentence starting with 'وقد يراد به معنى آخر' and its translation. Notice the key difference: the time is described as 'empty of the non-existence of the act'.
2
Contrast with the first meaning
In the first meaning, the time is conditioned as being before the act, which implies the act's non-existence. Here, the time is not conditioned by the act's non-existence; it is simply empty of it.
3
Understand the possibility
The author states that this meaning is not impossible in itself and does not entail any impossibility. This means the act could occur in such a time without contradiction.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This interpretation shows that the statement does not necessarily prove impossibility; it depends on how we understand 'before'. This clarifies the scope of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أي فيما ذكرناه من دليل الشيخ نظر يرجع ذلك النظر إلى تحقيق معنى قوله: حصول الفعل قبل الفعل محال. فإنه قد يراد به أن حصول الفعل في زمان بشرط كونه قبل الفعل محال فلا كلام فيه إذ لا شك أنه تناقض لاستلزامه أن يكون ذلك الزمان متقدماً على الفعل وأن لا يكون متقدماً عليه بل معه واستلزامه أيضاً اجتماع وجود الفعل وعدمه معاً، لكن هذا المحال لم يلزم من وجود الفعل في ذلك الزمان وحده حتى يلزم امتناعه فيه بل منه مع فرض كون ذلك الزمان قبل الفعل مقارناً لعدمه، فيكون هذا المجموع محالاً دون الفعل وحده، بل هو ممكن في ذاته قطعاً فلا يتصف بالامتناع الذاتي أصلاً، بل بالامتناع الغيري، وذلك لا ينافي تعلق القدرة به. وقد يراد به معنى آخر وهو وجود الفعل في زمان عدم الفعل لا بأن يجتمع فيه مع عدمه بل بأن يفرض خلوه عن عدم الفعل ويفرض وقوع الفعل فيه بدله وأنه غير محال في نفسه، ولا يستلزم محالاً أيضاً.
And in it, i.e., in what we mentioned of the proof of the Shaykh, there is a consideration; that consideration refers to the verification of the meaning of his saying: 'The occurrence of the act before the act is impossible.' For it may be intended by it that the occurrence of the act in a time with the condition of it being before the act is impossible, and there is no dispute about it, for there is no doubt that it is a contradiction, because it necessitates that that time be prior to the act and that it not be prior to it but rather with it, and also necessitates the conjunction of the existence of the act and its non-existence together. But this impossible thing did not follow from the existence of the act in that time alone such that its impossibility in it follows, but rather from it together with the assumption that that time is before the act concomitant with its non-existence. So this composite is impossible, not the act alone; rather it is possible in itself absolutely, so it is not characterized by intrinsic impossibility at all, but by extrinsic impossibility, and that does not negate the connection of power to it. And it may be intended by it another meaning, which is the existence of the act in a time of the non-existence of the act, not by it being together with its non-existence, but by supposing it empty of the non-existence of the act and supposing the occurrence of the act in its place, and that it is not impossible in itself and also does not entail anything impossible.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage provides an exegetical analysis of the statement 'The occurrence of the act before the act is impossible,' which is a key premise in the proof. The author distinguishes two possible meanings. The first meaning is that the act occurs in a time qualified as being before the act. This is indeed a contradiction because it requires the same time to be both before and simultaneous with the act, and also requires the act to exist and not exist together. However, this impossibility arises not from the act's existence in that time alone, but from the combination of the act's existence with the condition of being before the act. So the act itself is intrinsically possible, but extrinsically impossible due to the condition. This extrinsic impossibility does not prevent power from connecting to it. The second meaning is that the act exists in a time that is empty of its non-existence, i.e., a time that is not characterized by the act's absence. In this sense, the act's occurrence is not impossible in itself and does not entail any contradiction. This analysis is crucial for understanding the scope of the power's connection.

Key Terms
زمان عدم الفعل
خلو عن عدم الفعل
وقوع الفعل بدله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act cannot exist before its time under any interpretation.
Correction: The passage shows that if we consider a time empty of the act's non-existence, the act's occurrence is possible and does not entail contradiction.

Checkpoint
In the second meaning, how is the time described?
A) Conditioned as being before the act
B) Empty of the non-existence of the act
C) Characterized by the act's absence
D) Simultaneous with the act

Key Takeaway
The second meaning affirms the intrinsic possibility of the act in a time free from its non-existence, showing that the original statement does not prove impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself that something is impossible simply because it hasn't happened in your timeline? This lesson shows that intrinsic possibility is separate from temporal occurrence. Is there a promise of God you have stopped expecting because of the delay? Will you now reconsider it as intrinsically possible through His power?
?
The passage teaches that impossibility arises only from contradiction, not from timing alone. Where in your own life have you added a condition to God's power that He never placed? How can you remove that condition in your heart today?
?
If you truly believe that God can bring about an act in a time empty of its prior non-existence, what is one specific thing you will begin to ask Him for that you previously thought was impossible due to 'it's too late' or 'it's not the right time'? Write it down and commit to supplication.

Capability Before the Act and Invalidity of Commanding the Incapable
Defines capability and invalidates commanding the impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for Allah to command the creation of substances and accidents, and how the Mu'tazila's argument about excuses relies on rational good and evil which is later refuted.

Core Idea
What is impossible for a servant cannot be commanded, and the Mu'tazila's excuse argument rests on a flawed rationalist foundation.

This passage clarifies the scope of divine command. It distinguishes between acts a servant can perform or abandon and those he cannot, using the example of creating substances. It then indicates that the Mu'tazila rely on rational good and evil to argue for excuses, but that doctrine is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the contrast between two types of actions.
2
Identify the two cases
Identify the first case: abandoning an act by doing its opposite (disbelief) is possible for the servant. The second case: creating substances and accidents is impossible for the servant to do or leave.
This distinction determines the validity of a command.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila reference
Note that the passage mentions the Mu'tazila argument about excuses, which is based on rational good and evil. Recognize that this argument will be refuted later.
This reminds us that the author is engaged in a broader polemic.
4
Reflect on the key principle
Reflect on the principle that a command is only valid if the commanded act is within the servant's power. That is why commanding the creation of substances is invalid.
This principle is central to the Ash'ari position on divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدوراً له قبل حدوثه، لكن تركه بالتلبس بضده الذي هو الكفر مقدور له حال كونه كافراً بخلاف أحداث الجواهر والأعراض، فإنه غير مقدور له فعله ولا تركه، فلا يجوز التكليف به وأما ما ذكروه من قصة الأعذار ووجوب قبولها فمبني على قاعدة التحسين والتقبيح العقليين وسيأتي بطلانها.
It is possible for him before its occurrence, but its abandonment by engaging in its opposite which is disbelief is possible for him while he is a disbeliever, unlike the coming into existence of substances and accidents, for neither its action nor its abandonment is possible for him, so commanding it is not permissible. As for what they mentioned of the story of excuses and the obligation of accepting them, it is based on the rule of rational good and evil, and its falsity will come.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of commanded acts. It first states that an act can be possible before its occurrence, but leaving it by doing its opposite (disbelief) is possible for the person when he is a disbeliever. In contrast, creating substances and accidents is not possible for him to do or leave, so such a command (to create) is invalid. Then it refers to the Mu'tazila argument about excuses, noting it rests on rational good and evil, which will be refuted later.

Key Terms
قدرة
مقدور
تكليف
تحسين وتقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God commands impossible things, or that the passage suggests God commands the impossible.
Correction: The passage clarifies what is and isn't possible for a human agent, not for God. It shows that commanding the impossible is invalid because the servant lacks the power to comply.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible to command the creation of substances and accidents?
A) Because they are impossible for the servant.
B) Because they are possible but not good.
C) Because the command is too difficult.
D) Because the servant has power over them.

Key Takeaway
A command only applies to what a servant can do or leave; creating substances is beyond human power, so such a command is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses for your disobedience, do you realize that your ability to choose is not a license but a responsibility? Have you ever used the fact that you 'could have done otherwise' as an excuse to delay repentance? What would it mean to take responsibility for your choices today?
?
The passage says that creating substances is beyond your power. But your heart and actions are within your grasp. Are you using your ability to choose faith, or are you surrendering to disbelief? In what specific area of your life are you claiming powerlessness when Allah has given you ability?
?
The Mu‘tazila built their excuse argument on rational good and evil. Are you relying on your own reasoning to justify your faults, rather than submitting to Allah's command? What excuse have you been holding onto that you need to let go of right now?

The First Branch: Positions on Emptiness from All Possible Acts
Positions on emptiness from all possible acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing positions of Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i on whether an agent can be entirely free from all his possible acts, and the distinction between direct and generated acts.

Core Idea
Two scholars, two views: one allows absolute emptiness, the other distinguishes between direct and generated acts.

This lesson examines the first branch of the Mu'tazila concerning the agent's ability to be devoid of all possible actions. The debate hinges on whether power alone suffices for action or requires additional conditions. We will see the positions of Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main views
Read the passage to find the positions of Abu Hashim (absolute permissibility) and al-Jubba'i (conditional).
Notice that al-Jubba'i differentiates based on impediment and type of act.
2
Understand al-Jubba'i's distinction
Focus on the conditions: impediment presence vs absence, direct vs generated acts. See how this affects the permissibility of emptiness.
The distinction is key to understanding Mu'tazila nuance.
3
Grasp the role of temporal power
Note that the passage states temporal power is not free from relation to an act, which sets the stage for further discussion.
This connects to the broader theory of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الأول) هل يخلو القادر عن جميع مقدوراته جوزه أبو هاشم وأتباعه مطلقاً وفصل الجبائي فجوزه) أي الخلو عن جميع المقدورات ( عند ) وجود ( المانع ومنعه عند عدمه في المباشر دون المولد أي لم يجوز الخلو عند عدم المانع في الأفعال المباشرة، وجوزه في الأفعال المولدة، وقد تبين أن القدرة الحادثة لا تخلو عن قوله : ( على مذهبهم وهو لتعنتها على الفعل. قوله : (هل يخلو القادر عن جميع مقدوراته مع تحقق القدرة وتعلقها بناء على أن تعلق القدرة ليس علة تامة للواقع بل لا بد معه من ارتفاع المانع وتحقق الشرائط، فلا يرد ما قيل أن قاعدتهم من تقدم القدرة وتعلقها يقتضي جزم الكل لوقوع الخلو المذكور، وما قيل في الجواب عن الإيراد مرادهم جواز الخلو زماناً معتداً به، وتقدم القدرة على الفعل لا يستدعي لجواز كون التقدم أما سبق الفعل في الآن الثاني، فمع كونه بعيداً عن العبارة ينفيه ما سيجيء من أنهم اتفقوا على أنها لا تبقى غير متعلقة. قوله : ( ومنعه عند عدمه لتحقق المقتضي وارتفاع المانع فلا بد من تحقق المقدور قوله : ( دون المولدة لأن الأفعال المولدة قد لا تترتب على المباشرة كما في الضرب فإنه قد يولد الألم بعدم قابلية المحل. قوله : ( وقد تبين إلخ) فلا يصور هذا الاختلاف على مذهبهم. قوله : ( لكن تركه بالتلبس) قيل : جعل ترك الإيمان مقدوراً وعدم خلق الجواهر غير مقدور مع أن جانب الفعل غير مقدور في كل منهما تحكم، ولو قيل بمقدورية ترك الإيمان بناء على كون الإيمان مقدوراً في الجملة، لكونه صادراً عن بني نوعه بخلاف عدم الخلق لم يبعد فإن الأعراض عن الشيء بمعنى تركه يشعر بكونه بحيث يكون من شأن جنسه المقدورية فتأمل. قوله : (الأول) هل يخلو القادر إلخ حاصله : أنه هل يجوز أن يوجد القادر في وقت ولا يوجد فيه شيء من مقدوراته مع قطع النظر عن كونه قدرتها متعلقة بشيء منها أم لا، وقد يقال قاعدتهم تقتضي جزم الكل لجواز الخلو، بل وقوعه كما في أول زمان القدرة المتقدمة على الفعل عندهم ، اللهم إلا أن يقال مرادهم جواز الخلو زماناً ممتداً في الجملة وتقدم القدرة على الفعل لا يستدعيه لجواز كون التقدم آناً بأن يعقبه الفعل في الآن الثاني، فإن قلت : بعض المعتزلة وافقونا في أن القدرة مع الفعل وهم الذين لا يجوزون الخلو ومراد الشارح بمذهبهم في القدرة الحادثة القول بتأثيرها لا بتقدمها ؟ قلت: لا يلائمه السياق لأن الفروع فروع التقدم ولهذا ذكرت هذا المقصد كما لا يخفى.
(The first) Whether the agent is free from all his possible acts: Abu Hashim and his followers permitted it absolutely, and al-Jubba'i differentiated: he permitted it (i.e., freedom from all possible acts) in the presence of an impediment, and prevented it in its absence in direct acts but not in generated acts, i.e., he did not permit freedom when there is no impediment in direct acts, and permitted it in generated acts. And it has been shown that temporal power is not free from... His saying: 'according to their school, and it is their determination upon the act.' His saying: 'Whether the agent is free from all his possible acts upon the realization of power and its relation, based on the fact that the relation of power is not a sufficient cause for the actual, but rather must be accompanied by removal of impediment and realization of conditions. So what is said that their principle of the precedence of power and its relation necessitates the certainty of all for the occurrence of the mentioned freedom is not applicable. And what is said in answer to the objection: they mean the permissibility of freedom for a considerable time, and the precedence of power over the act does not necessitate that because the precedence could be the act preceding in the second now, and despite being far from the expression, it is negated by what will come that they agreed that it does not remain unrelated.' His saying: 'and prevented it in its absence due to the realization of the motive and removal of impediment, so the possible act must be realized.' His saying: 'except in generated acts because generated acts may not follow the direct act, as in hitting, for it may generate pain due to the lack of receptivity of the place.' His saying: 'and it has been shown, etc.' so this difference is not conceivable in their school. His saying: 'but its abandonment by engaging in [its opposite]' It is said: making the abandonment of faith possible and the non-creation of substances impossible, while the aspect of action is impossible in both, is arbitrary. And if it is said that the possibilty of abandoning faith is based on faith being possible in general, because it proceeds from his kind, unlike non-creation, it would not be far-fetched, for refraining from something meaning its abandonment suggests that it is such that its genus has possibility, so reflect. His saying: '(the first) whether the agent is free, etc.' Its summary: whether it is permissible that the agent exists at a time while none of his possible acts exist, regardless of whether his power is related to something or not. And it is said: their principle necessitates the certainty of all for the permissibility of freedom, rather its occurrence, as in the first moment of power preceding the act according to them. However, it is said: they mean the permissibility of freedom for an extended time in general, and the precedence of power over the act does not necessitate it because the precedence could be an instant such that the act follows in the second now. If you say: Some Mu'tazila agreed with us that power is with the act, and they are those who do not permit freedom, and the commentator's meaning by their school in temporal power is the view of its effectiveness not its precedence? I say: It does not suit the context because the branches are branches of precedence, and for this this objective was mentioned, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of the Mu'tazila concerning whether an agent can be devoid of all his possible acts. It states that Abu Hashim and his followers allow it absolutely, while al-Jubba'i distinguishes: he allows it when there is an impediment, and disallows it when there is no impediment in direct acts, but allows it in generated acts. The commentator then explains that the relation of power is not a sufficient cause; impediment removal is needed. He addresses an objection that the Mu'tazila principle of precedence of power would require emptiness to occur, and answers that they permit it for a considerable time, but the precedence may be momentary. Another objection distinguishes Mu'tazila who agree power is concurrent with act, but the author argues the branches are based on precedence. The passage then discusses the issue of making leaving faith possible while creating substances is impossible, concluding it is based on the possibility of the act in general.

Key Terms
قادر
مقدور
خلو
مانع
مباشرة
مولدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that power alone necessitates action, so emptiness is impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that power is not a sufficient cause; impediments and conditions also affect actuality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, when is it not permissible for the agent to be free from all possible acts?
A) When there is an impediment.
B) When there is no impediment in direct acts.
C) In generated acts.
D) In all cases.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila differ on whether an agent can be free from all acts; al-Jubba'i restricts this possibility based on impediments and type of act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you find that having the ability and motive to do good is enough, or are there hidden 'impediments' (e.g., laziness, distraction) that prevent you from acting? According to al-Jubba'i, when there is no impediment and the motive is present, the act must occur. If you fail to act despite ability and motive, what does that reveal about your heart's sincerity?
?
The lesson distinguishes between direct acts (like prayer) and generated acts (like the patience that prayer should produce). Do you ever focus only on the direct acts while neglecting the generated outcomes? For example, do you pray but still lack kindness? What generated acts are missing from your life, and why might they not be 'generating'?
?
Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i differed on whether an agent can ever be 'empty' of all acts. In your spiritual life, do you ever feel 'empty' of good deeds, even when you have the ability? What would al-Jubba'i say about the impediments that might be causing that emptiness, and how can you remove them?

The Rationale Behind the Distinction and the Example of Faith
Commentary on the branch and the example of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper reasoning behind al-Jubba'i's distinction and the implications of the example regarding abandoning faith versus non-creation.

Core Idea
The possibility of action depends not only on power but also on conditions, as shown by the debate on leaving faith.

This lesson continues the discussion on the first branch by examining the commentator's clarification that temporal power is not free, and then analyzes a critical example raised: why is abandoning faith possible but non-creation of substances not? This helps clarify the principles of power and possibility.

Guided Path
1
Examine the example of faith
Read the passage where it discusses why abandoning faith is considered possible while non-creation of substances is not.
Focus on the reasoning that the possibility of an act depends on its genus.
2
Understand the summary of the branch
The passage restates the core question: whether an agent can exist at a time without any of his possible acts.
This ties back to the initial branch.
3
Analyze the objection about precedence
The passage addresses an objection that the Mu'tazila principle implies emptiness must occur, and answers that it is only permissible for an extended time.
Note the distinction between momentary and extended emptiness.
4
Reflect on the final note about precedence
The commentator explains that these branches are based on the precedence of power, not on its effectiveness.
This clarifies the context of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الأول) هل يخلو القادر عن جميع مقدوراته جوزه أبو هاشم وأتباعه مطلقاً وفصل الجبائي فجوزه) أي الخلو عن جميع المقدورات ( عند ) وجود ( المانع ومنعه عند عدمه في المباشر دون المولد أي لم يجوز الخلو عند عدم المانع في الأفعال المباشرة، وجوزه في الأفعال المولدة، وقد تبين أن القدرة الحادثة لا تخلو عن قوله : ( على مذهبهم وهو لتعنتها على الفعل. قوله : (هل يخلو القادر عن جميع مقدوراته مع تحقق القدرة وتعلقها بناء على أن تعلق القدرة ليس علة تامة للواقع بل لا بد معه من ارتفاع المانع وتحقق الشرائط، فلا يرد ما قيل أن قاعدتهم من تقدم القدرة وتعلقها يقتضي جزم الكل لوقوع الخلو المذكور، وما قيل في الجواب عن الإيراد مرادهم جواز الخلو زماناً معتداً به، وتقدم القدرة على الفعل لا يستدعي لجواز كون التقدم أما سبق الفعل في الآن الثاني، فمع كونه بعيداً عن العبارة ينفيه ما سيجيء من أنهم اتفقوا على أنها لا تبقى غير متعلقة. قوله : ( ومنعه عند عدمه لتحقق المقتضي وارتفاع المانع فلا بد من تحقق المقدور قوله : ( دون المولدة لأن الأفعال المولدة قد لا تترتب على المباشرة كما في الضرب فإنه قد يولد الألم بعدم قابلية المحل. قوله : ( وقد تبين إلخ) فلا يصور هذا الاختلاف على مذهبهم. قوله : ( لكن تركه بالتلبس) قيل : جعل ترك الإيمان مقدوراً وعدم خلق الجواهر غير مقدور مع أن جانب الفعل غير مقدور في كل منهما تحكم، ولو قيل بمقدورية ترك الإيمان بناء على كون الإيمان مقدوراً في الجملة، لكونه صادراً عن بني نوعه بخلاف عدم الخلق لم يبعد فإن الأعراض عن الشيء بمعنى تركه يشعر بكونه بحيث يكون من شأن جنسه المقدورية فتأمل. قوله : (الأول) هل يخلو القادر إلخ حاصله : أنه هل يجوز أن يوجد القادر في وقت ولا يوجد فيه شيء من مقدوراته مع قطع النظر عن كونه قدرتها متعلقة بشيء منها أم لا، وقد يقال قاعدتهم تقتضي جزم الكل لجواز الخلو، بل وقوعه كما في أول زمان القدرة المتقدمة على الفعل عندهم ، اللهم إلا أن يقال مرادهم جواز الخلو زماناً ممتداً في الجملة وتقدم القدرة على الفعل لا يستدعيه لجواز كون التقدم آناً بأن يعقبه الفعل في الآن الثاني، فإن قلت : بعض المعتزلة وافقونا في أن القدرة مع الفعل وهم الذين لا يجوزون الخلو ومراد الشارح بمذهبهم في القدرة الحادثة القول بتأثيرها لا بتقدمها ؟ قلت: لا يلائمه السياق لأن الفروع فروع التقدم ولهذا ذكرت هذا المقصد كما لا يخفى.
(The first) Whether the agent is free from all his possible acts: Abu Hashim and his followers permitted it absolutely, and al-Jubba'i differentiated: he permitted it (i.e., freedom from all possible acts) in the presence of an impediment, and prevented it in its absence in direct acts but not in generated acts, i.e., he did not permit freedom when there is no impediment in direct acts, and permitted it in generated acts. And it has been shown that temporal power is not free from... His saying: 'according to their school, and it is their determination upon the act.' His saying: 'Whether the agent is free from all his possible acts upon the realization of power and its relation, based on the fact that the relation of power is not a sufficient cause for the actual, but rather must be accompanied by removal of impediment and realization of conditions. So what is said that their principle of the precedence of power and its relation necessitates the certainty of all for the occurrence of the mentioned freedom is not applicable. And what is said in answer to the objection: they mean the permissibility of freedom for a considerable time, and the precedence of power over the act does not necessitate that because the precedence could be the act preceding in the second now, and despite being far from the expression, it is negated by what will come that they agreed that it does not remain unrelated.' His saying: 'and prevented it in its absence due to the realization of the motive and removal of impediment, so the possible act must be realized.' His saying: 'except in generated acts because generated acts may not follow the direct act, as in hitting, for it may generate pain due to the lack of receptivity of the place.' His saying: 'and it has been shown, etc.' so this difference is not conceivable in their school. His saying: 'but its abandonment by engaging in [its opposite]' It is said: making the abandonment of faith possible and the non-creation of substances impossible, while the aspect of action is impossible in both, is arbitrary. And if it is said that the possibilty of abandoning faith is based on faith being possible in general, because it proceeds from his kind, unlike non-creation, it would not be far-fetched, for refraining from something meaning its abandonment suggests that it is such that its genus has possibility, so reflect. His saying: '(the first) whether the agent is free, etc.' Its summary: whether it is permissible that the agent exists at a time while none of his possible acts exist, regardless of whether his power is related to something or not. And it is said: their principle necessitates the certainty of all for the permissibility of freedom, rather its occurrence, as in the first moment of power preceding the act according to them. However, it is said: they mean the permissibility of freedom for an extended time in general, and the precedence of power over the act does not necessitate it because the precedence could be an instant such that the act follows in the second now. If you say: Some Mu'tazila agreed with us that power is with the act, and they are those who do not permit freedom, and the commentator's meaning by their school in temporal power is the view of its effectiveness not its precedence? I say: It does not suit the context because the branches are branches of precedence, and for this this objective was mentioned, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of the Mu'tazila concerning whether an agent can be devoid of all his possible acts. It states that Abu Hashim and his followers allow it absolutely, while al-Jubba'i distinguishes: he allows it when there is an impediment, and disallows it when there is no impediment in direct acts, but allows it in generated acts. The commentator then explains that the relation of power is not a sufficient cause; impediment removal is needed. He addresses an objection that the Mu'tazila principle of precedence of power would require emptiness to occur, and answers that they permit it for a considerable time, but the precedence may be momentary. Another objection distinguishes Mu'tazila who agree power is concurrent with act, but the author argues the branches are based on precedence. The passage then discusses the issue of making leaving faith possible while creating substances is impossible, concluding it is based on the possibility of the act in general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Mu'tazila unanimously agree on the possibility of emptiness.
Correction: The passage shows disagreement among Mu'tazila, and also that some hold power is concurrent with act, not preceding.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is abandoning faith considered possible while non-creation of substances is not?
A) Because faith is a direct act and non-creation is generated.
B) Because faith is possible from the agent's kind, unlike non-creation.
C) Because non-creation requires a special impediment.
D) Because the Mu'tazila do not discuss non-creation.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of emptiness from acts is debated, and the example of faith illustrates how possibility relates to the genus of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If abandoning faith is possible precisely because faith is from your human capacity, does your faith feel like a potential that you are actively actualizing, or something you take for granted? What one practical step can you take today to turn faith from a mere possibility into a lived reality?
?
The lesson shows that the possibility of your faith is not arbitrary—it is grounded in your very nature as a human being. How does this truth hold you accountable for your own belief? Are there areas where you neglect to cultivate your faith, assuming it is automatically present, while fearing its loss? What would change if you truly believed that your faith requires constant maintenance to avoid moving from possibility to actual abandonment?

The Mode of Power's Relation: Two Views
Describes the consensus on power's relation and two differing views on its temporal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus that power cannot exist unrelated and the two main views on how it relates across temporal conditions.

Core Idea
Power must relate to its act, but theologians disagree on when and how.

This lesson explores a key debate in kalam about the mode of relation between temporal power and its possible act. While all agree that power cannot be unrelated, they differ on whether it relates only to the immediately following moment or to all future moments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the consensus statement and the two views introduced by 'So it was said' and 'And it was said'.
The text at the end is corrupt, so focus on the clear parts.
2
Identify the two views
The first view says power relates only to the act in the 'second condition' (immediately after). The second view says it relates absolutely to all future conditions (second, third, etc.).
Notice the terminology of 'second condition', 'third condition', etc., which refer to consecutive moments.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it affects how we understand divine and human causality and the temporal relationship between power and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الثالث : اتفقوا على أنها لا تبقى غير متعلقة ) أي يستحيل أن توجد القدرة مع أنها لا تتعلق بمقدورها أصلا لكنهم اختلفوا في كيفية تعلقها به ( فقيل : ) القدرة ( الحادثة ( تتعلق بالفعل عقيبها أي هي في وقت وجودها متعلقة بالمقدور في الحالة الثانية فقط، فلا تتعلق في الحالة الثالثة إلا في الحالة الثانية وكذلك المقدور في الحالة الرابعة لا تتعلق به القدرة إلا في الحالة الثالثة وهكذا ( وقيل : ( القدرة حال وجودها متعلقة بما بعدها مطلقاً) أي هي في تلك الحالة متعلقة بوجود الفعل في الحالة الثانية والثالثة وما بعدها وليس يختص تعلقها بوجود الفعل في الحالة الثانية فقط، قال الآمدي : ثم إن المخصصين لتعلقها بالحالة قوله : ( أي يستحيل أن يوجد إلخ) أشار إلى أن المراد بالا مطلق الوجود. قوله: (فلا تتعلق به في الحالة الثالثة الظرف أعني في الحالة متعلق بضمير به متضمناً لمعنى الوجود كما أن قوله عقيبها ظرف للفعل لا المتعلق الفساد المعنى . لخونه
The third branch: They agreed that [power] does not remain unrelated, i.e., it is impossible that power exists while not being related to its possible act at all. But they disagreed on the mode of its relation to it. So it was said: The temporal power relates to the act after it; i.e., at the time of its existence it is related to the possible act in the second condition only. So it does not relate in the third condition except in the second condition, and likewise the possible act in the fourth condition is not related to power except in the third condition, and so on. And it was said: Power at the time of its existence is related to what is after it absolutely; i.e., in that condition it is related to the existence of the act in the second, third, and subsequent conditions, and its relation is not restricted to the existence of the act in the second condition only. Al-Amidi said: Then those who specify its relation to the condition... His saying: 'i.e., it is impossible that it exists, etc.' indicates that the intended meaning is absolute existence. His saying: 'so it does not relate to it in the third condition' – the adverb, i.e., 'in the condition' is attached to the pronoun 'it' containing the meaning of existence, just as his saying 'after it' is an adverb for the act, not for the related, corrupting the meaning. Lakhunah [؟].
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage addresses the third branch: the consensus that power cannot exist without being related to some possible act. The disagreement is on how it relates: one view says it relates only to the act in the immediately following moment (second condition), so it relates sequentially; another view says it relates to all future moments absolutely. Al-Amidi is then quoted, perhaps criticizing the first view. The commentator explains grammatical points about the phrasing, noting that 'in the condition' is attached to the pronoun implying existence, and that 'after it' is an adverb for the act, not for the relation, which would corrupt the meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power relates to its act at the exact same temporal moment, but the debate here is about a temporal gap: power exists in one condition and the act exists later.
Correction: The passage clarifies that power relates to future acts, not simultaneous ones.

Checkpoint
According to the first view on the mode of relation, power at the time of its existence relates to the possible act in which condition?
A) The first condition.
B) The second condition only.
C) The third condition.
D) All future conditions.

Key Takeaway
Theologians agree power cannot be unrelated, but they differ on whether it relates only to the immediate next moment or to all future moments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your power is never idle—it is always related to some act right now. What is your current power engaged with? Is it directed towards a good deed or wasted on distraction? How can you consciously direct your power to what pleases Allah in this very moment?
?
If your power only relates to the next act (first view), then you have no power over the distant future. Does this thought make you anxious or drive you to increase your trust in Allah, the only One with power over all times? How can you live with the awareness that you only influence the next moment?
?
The second view suggests your current power is related to all future acts. Do you live as if you control tomorrow? Or do you acknowledge that while your power intends future acts, it is Allah who brings them into existence? How does this nuance change your planning and reliance?

The Four Views on Heart and Limb Power
Sanusi presents four opinions on the scope of heart and limb power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four different opinions regarding whether the power of the heart and the power of the limbs can relate to each other's objects.

Core Idea
Power's scope depends on its instrument—heart power relates to beliefs, limb power to movements.

The author begins this section by stating the ruling: power with an act does not relate to opposites. He then reports four distinct views on the scope of heart and limb powers. This passage introduces the problem and sets the stage for the longer debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four views
Read the passage carefully to note each of the four statements about what heart power and limb power can relate to. They are each introduced by 'وقال'.
The first view distinguishes the two powers; the third mentions the instrument (آلة).
2
Notice the key terms: 'قدرة قلبية' and 'قدرة عضوية'
Identify how the author uses 'power of the heart' and 'organic power' to refer to two distinct types of power. Consider their different objects.
3
Understand the role of the instrument (آلة)
Reflect on the third view: the instrument (the body and its structure) is necessary for limb power to act. Why does heart power lack this instrument?
This connects to the later anatomical discussion.

النوع الرابع - المقصد السابع : القدرة مع الفعل لا تتعلق بالضدين

القدرة القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بجميع متعلقاتها كالاعتقادات والإرادات ونحوها دون القدرة القائمة بالجوارح فإنها لا تتعلق بجميع مقدوراتها من الاعتمادات والحركات وغيرها. وقال تارة أخرى: كل واحدة منهما تتعلق بجميع متعلقاتها دون متعلقات الأخرى. وقال تارة ثالثة: كل واحدة منهما تتعلق بمتعلقاتها التي هي أفعال القلوب والجوارح جميعاً غير أن كلاً منهما لا يؤثر في متعلقات الأخرى لعدم الآلة أي يمتنع إيجاد أفعال الجوارح بالقدرة القائمة بالقلب لعدم الآلات والبنية المخصوصة المناسبة لتلك الأفعال، وكذا العكس. وقال مرة رابعة: القدرة القلبية تتعلق بمتعلقاتهما معاً دون القدرة العضوية فإنها تتعلق بأفعال الجوارح دون على كون المكلف قادراً متمكناً من الفعل بنيت الدعوة إلى دين الحق والثواب والعقاب على الأفعال القلبية والقالبية وإذا ثبت تعلقها بالمتضادات فتعلقها بغيرها أولى، وأجيب عن ذلك بأنه إن أريد بكونه مضطراً أن فعله غير مقدور له فهو ممنوع، وإن أريد به أن مقدوره ومتعلق قدرته متعين وأنه لا مقدور له بهذه القدرة سواه فهذا ما ندعيه ونلتزمه ولا منازعة لنا في تسميته مضطراً، فإن الاضطرار بمعنى امتناع الانفكاك لا أن القادر على مع الشيء لا بد أن يكون قادراً على ضده قلنا جاز أن تكون القدرة المتعلقة بهما متعددة لا واحدة. قال الإمام الرازي: القدرة تطلق على مجرد القوة التي هي مبدأ للأفعال المختلفة الحيوانية وهي القوة العضلية التي هي بحيث متى انضم إليها إرادة أحد الضدين حصل ذلك الضد، ومتى انضم إليها إرادة الضد الآخر حصل ذلك الآخر ولا شك أن نسبتها أي نسبة هذه القوة إلى الضدين سواء وهي قبل الفعل والقدرة تطلق العلم بطريان السهو، وللمعتزلة عن هذا الإلزام اختلافات مذكورة في أبكار الأفكار لا يناسب ذكرها في هذا المختصر.
قوله: ( من جنس واحد من المقدورات على تعاقب الأزمنة أي الموجودة على التعاقب والمراد بالجنس النوع فإن الجنس يطلق لغة على النوع كما أشار إليه الشارح في ثالث مباحث الحرارة فإن قلت المتماثلات لا تكون إلا من جنس واحد إذ التماثل كما سبق هو الاتحاد في النوع فما فائدة قول الشارح من جنس واحد؟ قلت: فائدته هي الإشارة إلى أن تماثل المتماثلات التي جوز المعتزلة تعلق القدرة بها بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الجميع لا بالنسبة من واحد إلى آخر ومن ثالث بالنسبة إلى رابع فليس مرادهم جواز تعلق القدرة الواحدة بهذه الحركة وتلك الحركة وبهذا السكون ذلك السكون أيضاً مع وجود التماثل بين الحركتين والسكونين.

قوله: ( وتركه ) عطف تفسيري لعدم الفعل إشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد منه عدم الفعل الأزلي بل تركه سواء فسر الترك بكف النفس أو بعدم الفعل قصداً.

قوله: (وهي القوة الخ) اعلم أنه نبت من الدماغ والنخاع عصب ومن العظم الذي يراد حركة رباط، فإذا التقى العصب والرباط تشيا شظايا دقاقاً، ويحشى الفرج الواقعة بينهما لحماً آخر ويخلل الغشاء، ويسمى ذلك الجسم المركب من العصب والرباط الجسم الأحمر، والغشاء عضلة ثم ما يبرز من العصب والرباط ينتقل فيصير جسماً واحداً في الحس متوسطاً بين لين العصب، وصلابة الرباط ويسمى الوتر وفائدتها أن يتحدب عند عقاص العضلة فينقبض العضد المراد تحريك وليترخى عند انبساط عائدة إلى وصفها الأولى، أو زائدة منه على مقدارها في طولها حال كونها على وضع المطلوب فيه العضو.

قوله: ( وهي القوة العضلية ) قال في بحث الحروف المصوتة من شرح الملخص: العصب جسم منبته الدماغ والنخاع وهو أبيض لين في الانعطاف صلب في الانفصال خلق ليتم به للأعضاء الحس والحركة والرباط جسم شبيه بالعصب، وإذا عرفت العصب والرباط فاعلم أن العضلة جرم مؤلف من العصب والرباط ومن ليفهما، ومن اللحم الحاشي في خللها والعشاء لها، إذا تصلب حدث الوتر وهو الملتئم من الرباط والعصب النافذ منها إلى جانب العضو وهي فيتشنج فتجذب العضو، وإذا انبسطت استرخى الوتر فتباعد العضو.
Chapter Four - Seventh Aim: Power with an act does not relate to opposites. The power residing in the heart relates to all its objects such as beliefs and volitions and the like, but not the power residing in the limbs, for it does not relate to all of its possibles from intentions and movements and others. And he said another time: Each one of them relates to all its objects without the objects of the other. And he said a third time: Each one of them relates to its objects which are the actions of the hearts and limbs all together, except that each of them does not effect the objects of the other due to the lack of the instrument, meaning it is impossible to produce the actions of the limbs by the power residing in the heart due to the lack of instruments and the specific structure suitable for those actions, and likewise the opposite. And he said a fourth time: The heart's power relates to both of their objects together, but not the organic power, for it relates to the actions of the limbs without the actions of the hearts. And Ibn al-Rawandi from the Mu'tazila and many of our companions said: The created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. And the Mu'tazila unanimously agreed that it, i.e., the single power, relates to similar things of one genus from the possibles over successive times and seasons, with all of them agreeing that no two similar acts occur by it, i.e., that single power, in one place at one time, and that they, i.e., the Mu'tazila, claim in what they held, i.e., the relation of power to opposites, necessity, for there is no meaning to power except capability of the two ends, i.e., the two ends of the possible act, and whoever is not capable of the non-existence of the act and its abandonment which is its opposite and contradictory is compelled and forced to the act such that he cannot separate from it, not capable of it, and it is false. How? And upon it, i.e., upon the obligated being powerful and capable of the act, the call to the religion of truth and reward and punishment for the actions of the hearts and bodies is built. And if its relation to opposites is established, then its relation to non-opposites is more primary. And it was answered about that that if by 'compelled' it is meant that his act is not possible for him, then it is denied; and if by it is meant that his possible and the object of his power is specified and that he has no possible by this power other than it, then this is what we claim and adhere to, and there is no dispute for us in calling him compelled, for compulsion in the sense of impossibility of separation does not contradict power. Do you not see that one who is surrounded by a building from all his sides such that he is incapable of moving from one direction to another, is capable of his location by consensus from us and from them, and he has no way to separate from his possible. Al-Amidi said: And even if we concede that the one who is powerful over a thing must be powerful over its opposite, we say it is possible that the power related to them is multiple, not one. Imam al-Razi said: Power is applied to mere force which is a principle for the various animal actions, and it is the muscular power which is such that when the will for one of the two opposites is joined to it, that opposite occurs, and when the will for the other opposite is joined to it, that other occurs, and there is no doubt that its relation, i.e., the relation of this force, to the two opposites is equal, and it is before the act. And power is also applied to the force that has gathered all the conditions for effect, without doubt.

His saying: ('such as beliefs') which are theoretical and possible.
His saying: ('and also volitions') which are possible and others from the attribution of acceptance occurring by choice.
His saying: ('from one kind' i.e., one species, because similar things are of two species, like similar movements and similar rest.
Knowledge of the occurrence of forgetfulness, and for the Mu'tazila regarding this objection there are differences mentioned in Abkar al-Afkar, and it is not appropriate to mention them in this abridged work.
His saying: ('from one kind of the possibles over successive times') i.e., existing successively, and by 'kind' is meant 'species', for 'kind' is applied linguistically to 'species', as the commentator indicated in the third investigation of heat. For if you say: Similar things are only from one species, since similarity, as previously mentioned, is unity in species, then what is the benefit of the commentator's saying 'from one kind'? I say: Its benefit is to indicate that the similarity of the similar things which the Mu'tazila allowed the attachment of power to, is relative to each one of the whole, not relative from one to another and from a third relative to a fourth. So their intention is not the permissibility of the single power attaching to this movement and that movement and to this rest and that rest also, with the existence of similarity between the two movements and the two rests.

His saying: ('and its abandonment') is an explicative conjunction for 'non-existence of the act', indicating that what is meant by it is not the eternal non-existence of the act but its abandonment, whether abandonment is interpreted as refraining the soul or as intentional non-existence of the act.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force'): Know that from the brain and spinal cord a nerve grows, and from the bone which is intended for movement a ligament. When the nerve and ligament meet, they form into fine shreds, and the gaps between them are filled with another flesh, and it is interwoven with the membrane, and that composite body of nerve and ligament is called the red body, and the membrane is a muscle. Then what emerges from the nerve and ligament transfers and becomes one body in sensation, intermediate between the softness of the nerve and the hardness of the ligament, and is called the tendon. Its benefit is that it bends at the knot of the muscle, so the arm intended for movement contracts, and it relaxes upon extension returning to its first description, or it extends from it upon its measure in its length when it is in the desired position of the limb.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force') – He said in the investigation of voiced letters from Sharh al-Mulakhkhas: The nerve is a body whose origin is the brain and spinal cord, and it is white, soft in bending, hard in separating; it was created to give the limbs sensation and movement. The ligament is a body similar to the nerve. When you know the nerve and ligament, know that the muscle is a composite of nerve and ligament and their fibers, and of the stuffing flesh in its interstices and the membrane covering it. When it hardens, the tendon occurs, which is the fusion of the ligament and the nerve extending from it to the side of the limb, and it contracts, thereby pulling the limb; and when it expands, the tendon relaxes so the limb moves away.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion on whether a single power (qudra) can relate to two opposite actions. The author begins by stating the topic: 'Power with an act does not relate to opposites.' He then presents several opinions. First, he reports a view distinguishing between heart-power and limb-power: heart-power relates to all its objects (beliefs, volitions), while limb-power relates only to some physical objects like movements. A second view says each power relates to all its own objects exclusively. A third view says each power relates to all objects of both categories but cannot effect the other's objects due to lacking the proper instrument (āla). A fourth view says heart-power relates to both categories whereas organic (limb-)power relates only to physical acts. Then Ibn al-Rawandi and many Ash'ari scholars hold that created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. The Mu'tazila unanimously agree that a single power relates to similar things (mutamāthilāt) of one type over successive times, and they all agree that one power cannot produce two similar acts at the same time. They claim as a necessity (ḍarūra) that power is essentially ability to perform both ends of an act; otherwise the agent would be compelled (muḍṭarr). The author refutes this by noting that the entire religious law—call to truth, reward, and punishment—is built on the assumption that the obligated person is genuinely capable. Then follows an answer: if by 'compelled' they mean the act is impossible for him, that is denied; if they mean his power is restricted to one specific act (not two opposites), we accept that, and it does not negate power. He gives an example: a man surrounded by walls is capable of staying in his place even though he cannot leave. Al-Āmidī argues that even if we concede that power must relate to both opposites, it could be multiple powers, not one. Al-Rāzī distinguishes two meanings of power: (1) pure force (quwwa) that is a principle for various animal actions and is equally related to opposites before the act, and (2) the force that has all conditions for effecting an action. The passage also includes detailed glosses explaining key terms: 'beliefs' means theoretical possibles; 'volitions' means possibles chosen; 'from one kind' specifies that similarity is relative to each individual, not cross-category. A note mentions that the Mu'tazila have various answers to the objection of forgetfulness, but they are not mentioned here. The anatomical commentary at the end explains the physical basis of muscular power, describing nerves, ligaments, muscles, and tendons, with their roles in movement.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
قلب (qalb)
جوارح (jawāriḥ)
آلة (āla)

Checkpoint
According to the third opinion, why does heart power not affect limb actions?
A) Because heart power is weaker.
B) Because heart power lacks the appropriate instrument (āla).
C) Because limb power is superior.
D) Because heart power only relates to beliefs.

Key Takeaway
The four views show early disagreement on whether power is specific to its domain or universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four opinions on the scope of heart and limb power resonates most with your own experience of faith? Why do you lean toward that view, and what does that reveal about how you understand the connection between your inner convictions and your outward actions?
?
If heart power truly does not relate to limb movements, then how do you explain the times when your heart's belief has directly influenced your physical deeds? Are those moments evidence against the first opinion, or proof of something else? Be honest with yourself.
?
Imagine tomorrow you woke up with a paralyzed limb but a sound heart. According to the fourth opinion, your heart power would still relate to limb actions, but your limb power would not. Would you still be responsible for performing outward acts of worship? What does your answer say about whether you truly accept the authority of this theological framework over your life?

The Mu'tazila Argument from Necessity and the Ash'ari Response
The Mu'tazila assert power must relate to opposites; the response clarifies the nature of compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that a single power must be able to produce opposites, and the Ash'ari counterargument that restricted power does not imply compulsion.

Core Idea
Power does not require ability to do both opposite acts; a restricted capacity is still genuine power.

In this passage, the Mu'tazila argue from the definition of power as 'ability to do both ends' to claim that a single power must relate to opposites. The author responds by distinguishing between compulsion in the sense of impossibility and compulsion in the sense of restricted possibility. An illustrative example is given of a person surrounded by walls.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position and their claim of necessity
Identify the Mu'tazila argument: power is essentially the ability to perform both the act and its opposite. If not, the agent would be compelled.
Notice the phrase 'لا معنى للقدرة إلا التمكن من الطرفين'.
2
Study the response and the building example
Read the answer that distinguishes two meanings of 'compelled' and consider the example of a man imprisoned by walls who is still capable of staying in place.
The example shows that being unable to do the opposite does not negate power over the actual act.
3
Reflect on moral responsibility
Consider how this argument supports the basis of religious obligations: reward and punishment require genuine capability, not unrestricted capability.

Chapter Four - Seventh Aim: Power with an act does not relate to opposites. The power residing in the heart relates to all its objects such as beliefs and volitions and the like, but not the power residing in the limbs, for it does not relate to all of its possibles from intentions and movements and others. And he said another time: Each one of them relates to all its objects without the objects of the other. And he said a third time: Each one of them relates to its objects which are the actions of the hearts and limbs all together, except that each of them does not effect the objects of the other due to the lack of the instrument, meaning it is impossible to produce the actions of the limbs by the power residing in the heart due to the lack of instruments and the specific structure suitable for those actions, and likewise the opposite. And he said a fourth time: The heart's power relates to both of their objects together, but not the organic power, for it relates to the actions of the limbs without the actions of the hearts. And Ibn al-Rawandi from the Mu'tazila and many of our companions said: The created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. And the Mu'tazila unanimously agreed that it, i.e., the single power, relates to similar things of one genus from the possibles over successive times and seasons, with all of them agreeing that no two similar acts occur by it, i.e., that single power, in one place at one time, and that they, i.e., the Mu'tazila, claim in what they held, i.e., the relation of power to opposites, necessity, for there is no meaning to power except capability of the two ends, i.e., the two ends of the possible act, and whoever is not capable of the non-existence of the act and its abandonment which is its opposite and contradictory is compelled and forced to the act such that he cannot separate from it, not capable of it, and it is false. How? And upon it, i.e., upon the obligated being powerful and capable of the act, the call to the religion of truth and reward and punishment for the actions of the hearts and bodies is built. And if its relation to opposites is established, then its relation to non-opposites is more primary. And it was answered about that that if by 'compelled' it is meant that his act is not possible for him, then it is denied; and if by it is meant that his possible and the object of his power is specified and that he has no possible by this power other than it, then this is what we claim and adhere to, and there is no dispute for us in calling him compelled, for compulsion in the sense of impossibility of separation does not contradict power. Do you not see that one who is surrounded by a building from all his sides such that he is incapable of moving from one direction to another, is capable of his location by consensus from us and from them, and he has no way to separate from his possible. Al-Amidi said: And even if we concede that the one who is powerful over a thing must be powerful over its opposite, we say it is possible that the power related to them is multiple, not one. Imam al-Razi said: Power is applied to mere force which is a principle for the various animal actions, and it is the muscular power which is such that when the will for one of the two opposites is joined to it, that opposite occurs, and when the will for the other opposite is joined to it, that other occurs, and there is no doubt that its relation, i.e., the relation of this force, to the two opposites is equal, and it is before the act. And power is also applied to the force that has gathered all the conditions for effect, without doubt.

His saying: ('such as beliefs') which are theoretical and possible.
His saying: ('and also volitions') which are possible and others from the attribution of acceptance occurring by choice.
His saying: ('from one kind' i.e., one species, because similar things are of two species, like similar movements and similar rest.
Knowledge of the occurrence of forgetfulness, and for the Mu'tazila regarding this objection there are differences mentioned in Abkar al-Afkar, and it is not appropriate to mention them in this abridged work.
His saying: ('from one kind of the possibles over successive times') i.e., existing successively, and by 'kind' is meant 'species', for 'kind' is applied linguistically to 'species', as the commentator indicated in the third investigation of heat. For if you say: Similar things are only from one species, since similarity, as previously mentioned, is unity in species, then what is the benefit of the commentator's saying 'from one kind'? I say: Its benefit is to indicate that the similarity of the similar things which the Mu'tazila allowed the attachment of power to, is relative to each one of the whole, not relative from one to another and from a third relative to a fourth. So their intention is not the permissibility of the single power attaching to this movement and that movement and to this rest and that rest also, with the existence of similarity between the two movements and the two rests.

His saying: ('and its abandonment') is an explicative conjunction for 'non-existence of the act', indicating that what is meant by it is not the eternal non-existence of the act but its abandonment, whether abandonment is interpreted as refraining the soul or as intentional non-existence of the act.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force'): Know that from the brain and spinal cord a nerve grows, and from the bone which is intended for movement a ligament. When the nerve and ligament meet, they form into fine shreds, and the gaps between them are filled with another flesh, and it is interwoven with the membrane, and that composite body of nerve and ligament is called the red body, and the membrane is a muscle. Then what emerges from the nerve and ligament transfers and becomes one body in sensation, intermediate between the softness of the nerve and the hardness of the ligament, and is called the tendon. Its benefit is that it bends at the knot of the muscle, so the arm intended for movement contracts, and it relaxes upon extension returning to its first description, or it extends from it upon its measure in its length when it is in the desired position of the limb.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force') – He said in the investigation of voiced letters from Sharh al-Mulakhkhas: The nerve is a body whose origin is the brain and spinal cord, and it is white, soft in bending, hard in separating; it was created to give the limbs sensation and movement. The ligament is a body similar to the nerve. When you know the nerve and ligament, know that the muscle is a composite of nerve and ligament and their fibers, and of the stuffing flesh in its interstices and the membrane covering it. When it hardens, the tendon occurs, which is the fusion of the ligament and the nerve extending from it to the side of the limb, and it contracts, thereby pulling the limb; and when it expands, the tendon relaxes so the limb moves away.

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion on whether a single power (qudra) can relate to two opposite actions. The author begins by stating the topic: 'Power with an act does not relate to opposites.' He then presents several opinions. First, he reports a view distinguishing between heart-power and limb-power: heart-power relates to all its objects (beliefs, volitions), while limb-power relates only to some physical objects like movements. A second view says each power relates to all its own objects exclusively. A third view says each power relates to all objects of both categories but cannot effect the other's objects due to lacking the proper instrument (āla). A fourth view says heart-power relates to both categories whereas organic (limb-)power relates only to physical acts. Then Ibn al-Rawandi and many Ash'ari scholars hold that created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. The Mu'tazila unanimously agree that a single power relates to similar things (mutamāthilāt) of one type over successive times, and they all agree that one power cannot produce two similar acts at the same time. They claim as a necessity (ḍarūra) that power is essentially ability to perform both ends of an act; otherwise the agent would be compelled (muḍṭarr). The author refutes this by noting that the entire religious law—call to truth, reward, and punishment—is built on the assumption that the obligated person is genuinely capable. Then follows an answer: if by 'compelled' they mean the act is impossible for him, that is denied; if they mean his power is restricted to one specific act (not two opposites), we accept that, and it does not negate power. He gives an example: a man surrounded by walls is capable of staying in his place even though he cannot leave. Al-Āmidī argues that even if we concede that power must relate to both opposites, it could be multiple powers, not one. Al-Rāzī distinguishes two meanings of power: (1) pure force (quwwa) that is a principle for various animal actions and is equally related to opposites before the act, and (2) the force that has all conditions for effecting an action. The passage also includes detailed glosses explaining key terms: 'beliefs' means theoretical possibles; 'volitions' means possibles chosen; 'from one kind' specifies that similarity is relative to each individual, not cross-category. A note mentions that the Mu'tazila have various answers to the objection of forgetfulness, but they are not mentioned here. The anatomical commentary at the end explains the physical basis of muscular power, describing nerves, ligaments, muscles, and tendons, with their roles in movement.

Key Terms
ضدين (diddayn)
ضرورة (ḍarūra)
مضطر (muḍṭarr)
متماثلات (mutamāthilāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A power that cannot produce its opposite is not genuine power; the agent must be compelled.
Correction: Power can be restricted to one specific act, and the agent is still genuinely powerful over that act, as shown by the building example.

Checkpoint
What does the example of a man surrounded by a building demonstrate?
A) That power always relates to opposites.
B) That being unable to leave does not negate power over one's current location.
C) That the man is compelled to move.
D) That the building is an instrument.

Key Takeaway
A power can be genuinely effective even if it can only produce one specific act, as long as that act is within the agent's control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a habit or action you repeat daily that you tell yourself you 'have no choice' about—like a sin or a neglect of duty. According to the Ash'ari response in this lesson, is it possible that you actually have genuine power over that specific act? Will you today act on that power and choose obedience, even if you cannot imagine the opposite?
?
The man in the building is surrounded by walls—he cannot leave but is fully capable of staying. Are there 'walls' in your life—circumstances, pressures, or addictions—that you use as an excuse for not obeying Allah? How does this lesson redefine your agency within those walls? Write one concrete step you will take today that acknowledges your real capacity.
?
If you truly believed that your power is real even when limited to one specific good act, how would that change the way you approach a difficult command of Allah that you have been avoiding? Commit to performing that act tomorrow, not as a test of ability but as an acknowledgment that you are already capable.

intermediate

Al-Amidi and Al-Razi on the Multiplicity and Two Senses of Power
Al-Amidi suggests multiple powers; al-Razi defines two senses of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's concession that power could be multiple and al-Razi's distinction between power as mere force and power as complete capacity.

Core Idea
Power can be multiple powers, and the word 'power' has two distinct meanings.

After the main exchange, al-Amidi offers a different approach: even if power must relate to opposites, it could be multiple powers. Then al-Razi introduces a crucial distinction between power as a general force (quwwa) and power as a fully equipped capacity (qudra). This refines the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the quotations from al-Amidi and al-Razi
Notice al-Amidi's hypothetical concession and his suggestion that multiple powers could be involved. Then read al-Razi's two definitions of 'power'.
Al-Amidi says: 'جاز أن تكون القدرة متعلقة بهما متعددة لا واحدة'.
2
Understand al-Razi's first definition: power as mere force
This is the principle (mabda') of various animal actions, equally related to opposites before the act. It is called 'quwwa' or muscular force.
This sense does not guarantee the act will occur; it requires will.
3
Understand al-Razi's second definition: power as complete capacity
This is the force that has gathered all conditions for effect (sharā'iṭ al-ta'thīr). This sense implies the act will happen if nothing prevents.
This is closer to the theological term 'qudra'.
