النوع الرابع - المقصد السابع : القدرة مع الفعل لا تتعلق بالضدين

القدرة القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بجميع متعلقاتها كالاعتقادات والإرادات ونحوها دون القدرة القائمة بالجوارح فإنها لا تتعلق بجميع مقدوراتها من الاعتمادات والحركات وغيرها. وقال تارة أخرى: كل واحدة منهما تتعلق بجميع متعلقاتها دون متعلقات الأخرى. وقال تارة ثالثة: كل واحدة منهما تتعلق بمتعلقاتها التي هي أفعال القلوب والجوارح جميعاً غير أن كلاً منهما لا يؤثر في متعلقات الأخرى لعدم الآلة أي يمتنع إيجاد أفعال الجوارح بالقدرة القائمة بالقلب لعدم الآلات والبنية المخصوصة المناسبة لتلك الأفعال، وكذا العكس. وقال مرة رابعة: القدرة القلبية تتعلق بمتعلقاتهما معاً دون القدرة العضوية فإنها تتعلق بأفعال الجوارح دون أفعال القلوب. وقال ابن الراوندي من المعتزلة وكثير من أصحابنا: تتعلق القدرة الحادثة بالضدين بدلاً لا معاً. وأجمعت المعتزلة على أنها أي القدرة الواحدة تتعلق بالمتماثلات من جنس واحد من المقدورات على تعاقب الأزمنة والأوقات مع اتفاقهم بأسرهم على أنه لا يقع بها أي بتلك القدرة الواحدة مثلان في محل واحد في وقت واحد وأنهم أي المعتزلة يدعون فيما ذهبوا إليه من تعلق القدرة بالضدين الضرورة إذ لا معنى للقدرة إلا التمكن من الطرفين أي طرفي الفعل المقدور ومن لا يكون قادراً على عدم الفعل وتركه الذي هو ضده ومنافيه فهو مضطر وملجأ إلى الفعل بحيث لا يقدر على الانفكاك عنه لا قادر عليه وهو باطل كيف وعليه أي على كون المكلف قادراً متمكناً من الفعل بنيت الدعوة إلى دين الحق والثواب والعقاب على الأفعال القلبية والقالبية وإذا ثبت تعلقها بالمتضادات فتعلقها بغيرها أولى، وأجيب عن ذلك بأنه إن أريد بكونه مضطراً أن فعله غير مقدور له فهو ممنوع، وإن أريد به أن مقدوره ومتعلق قدرته متعين وأنه لا مقدور له بهذه القدرة سواه فهذا ما ندعيه ونلتزمه ولا منازعة لنا في تسميته مضطراً، فإن الاضطرار بمعنى امتناع الانفكاك لا ينافي القدرة ألا ترى أن من أحاط به بناء من جميع جوانبه بحيث يعجز التقلب من من جهة إلى أخرى، فإنه قادر على الكون في مكانه بإجماع منا ومنهم، أنه لا سبيل له إلى الانفكاك عن مقدوره. قال الآمدي ولئن سلمنا أن القادر على مع الشيء لا بد أن يكون قادراً على ضده قلنا جاز أن تكون القدرة المتعلقة بهما متعددة لا واحدة. قال الإمام الرازي: القدرة تطلق على مجرد القوة التي هي مبدأ للأفعال المختلفة الحيوانية وهي القوة العضلية التي هي بحيث متى انضم إليها إرادة أحد الضدين حصل ذلك الضد، ومتى انضم إليها إرادة الضد الآخر حصل ذلك الآخر ولا شك أن نسبتها أي نسبة هذه القوة إلى الضدين سواء وهي قبل الفعل والقدرة تطلق أيضاً على القوة المستجمعة لشرائط التأثير برمتها ولا شك

قوله: (كالاعتقادات) ذات النظرية المقدورة.
قوله: (وكذا الإرادات) المقدورة وغيرها من اتصاف القبلية الحاصلة بالاختيار.
قوله: (من جنس واحد أي من نوع واحد لأن المتماثلات من نوعين كالحركات المتماثلة والسكون المتماثلة.
العلم بطريان السهو، وللمعتزلة عن هذا الإلزام اختلافات مذكورة في أبكار الأفكار لا يناسب ذكرها في هذا المختصر.
قوله: ( من جنس واحد من المقدورات على تعاقب الأزمنة أي الموجودة على التعاقب والمراد بالجنس النوع فإن الجنس يطلق لغة على النوع كما أشار إليه الشارح في ثالث مباحث الحرارة فإن قلت المتماثلات لا تكون إلا من جنس واحد إذ التماثل كما سبق هو الاتحاد في النوع فما فائدة قول الشارح من جنس واحد؟ قلت: فائدته هي الإشارة إلى أن تماثل المتماثلات التي جوز المعتزلة تعلق القدرة بها بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الجميع لا بالنسبة من واحد إلى آخر ومن ثالث بالنسبة إلى رابع فليس مرادهم جواز تعلق القدرة الواحدة بهذه الحركة وتلك الحركة وبهذا السكون ذلك السكون أيضاً مع وجود التماثل بين الحركتين والسكونين.

قوله: ( وتركه ) عطف تفسيري لعدم الفعل إشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد منه عدم الفعل الأزلي بل تركه سواء فسر الترك بكف النفس أو بعدم الفعل قصداً.

قوله: (وهي القوة الخ) اعلم أنه نبت من الدماغ والنخاع عصب ومن العظم الذي يراد حركة رباط، فإذا التقى العصب والرباط تشيا شظايا دقاقاً، ويحشى الفرج الواقعة بينهما لحماً آخر ويخلل الغشاء، ويسمى ذلك الجسم المركب من العصب والرباط الجسم الأحمر، والغشاء عضلة ثم ما يبرز من العصب والرباط ينتقل فيصير جسماً واحداً في الحس متوسطاً بين لين العصب، وصلابة الرباط ويسمى الوتر وفائدتها أن يتحدب عند عقاص العضلة فينقبض العضد المراد تحريك وليترخى عند انبساط عائدة إلى وصفها الأولى، أو زائدة منه على مقدارها في طولها حال كونها على وضع المطلوب فيه العضو.

قوله: ( وهي القوة العضلية ) قال في بحث الحروف المصوتة من شرح الملخص: العصب جسم منبته الدماغ والنخاع وهو أبيض لين في الانعطاف صلب في الانفصال خلق ليتم به للأعضاء الحس والحركة والرباط جسم شبيه بالعصب، وإذا عرفت العصب والرباط فاعلم أن العضلة جرم مؤلف من العصب والرباط ومن ليفهما، ومن اللحم الحاشي في خللها والعشاء لها، إذا تصلب حدث الوتر وهو الملتئم من الرباط والعصب النافذ منها إلى جانب العضو وهي فيتشنج فتجذب العضو، وإذا انبسطت استرخى الوتر فتباعد العضو.
Chapter Four - Seventh Aim: Power with an act does not relate to opposites. The power residing in the heart relates to all its objects such as beliefs and volitions and the like, but not the power residing in the limbs, for it does not relate to all of its possibles from intentions and movements and others. And he said another time: Each one of them relates to all its objects without the objects of the other. And he said a third time: Each one of them relates to its objects which are the actions of the hearts and limbs all together, except that each of them does not effect the objects of the other due to the lack of the instrument, meaning it is impossible to produce the actions of the limbs by the power residing in the heart due to the lack of instruments and the specific structure suitable for those actions, and likewise the opposite. And he said a fourth time: The heart's power relates to both of their objects together, but not the organic power, for it relates to the actions of the limbs without the actions of the hearts. And Ibn al-Rawandi from the Mu'tazila and many of our companions said: The created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. And the Mu'tazila unanimously agreed that it, i.e., the single power, relates to similar things of one genus from the possibles over successive times and seasons, with all of them agreeing that no two similar acts occur by it, i.e., that single power, in one place at one time, and that they, i.e., the Mu'tazila, claim in what they held, i.e., the relation of power to opposites, necessity, for there is no meaning to power except capability of the two ends, i.e., the two ends of the possible act, and whoever is not capable of the non-existence of the act and its abandonment which is its opposite and contradictory is compelled and forced to the act such that he cannot separate from it, not capable of it, and it is false. How? And upon it, i.e., upon the obligated being powerful and capable of the act, the call to the religion of truth and reward and punishment for the actions of the hearts and bodies is built. And if its relation to opposites is established, then its relation to non-opposites is more primary. And it was answered about that that if by 'compelled' it is meant that his act is not possible for him, then it is denied; and if by it is meant that his possible and the object of his power is specified and that he has no possible by this power other than it, then this is what we claim and adhere to, and there is no dispute for us in calling him compelled, for compulsion in the sense of impossibility of separation does not contradict power. Do you not see that one who is surrounded by a building from all his sides such that he is incapable of moving from one direction to another, is capable of his location by consensus from us and from them, and he has no way to separate from his possible. Al-Amidi said: And even if we concede that the one who is powerful over a thing must be powerful over its opposite, we say it is possible that the power related to them is multiple, not one. Imam al-Razi said: Power is applied to mere force which is a principle for the various animal actions, and it is the muscular power which is such that when the will for one of the two opposites is joined to it, that opposite occurs, and when the will for the other opposite is joined to it, that other occurs, and there is no doubt that its relation, i.e., the relation of this force, to the two opposites is equal, and it is before the act. And power is also applied to the force that has gathered all the conditions for effect, without doubt.

His saying: ('such as beliefs') which are theoretical and possible.
His saying: ('and also volitions') which are possible and others from the attribution of acceptance occurring by choice.
His saying: ('from one kind' i.e., one species, because similar things are of two species, like similar movements and similar rest.
Knowledge of the occurrence of forgetfulness, and for the Mu'tazila regarding this objection there are differences mentioned in Abkar al-Afkar, and it is not appropriate to mention them in this abridged work.
His saying: ('from one kind of the possibles over successive times') i.e., existing successively, and by 'kind' is meant 'species', for 'kind' is applied linguistically to 'species', as the commentator indicated in the third investigation of heat. For if you say: Similar things are only from one species, since similarity, as previously mentioned, is unity in species, then what is the benefit of the commentator's saying 'from one kind'? I say: Its benefit is to indicate that the similarity of the similar things which the Mu'tazila allowed the attachment of power to, is relative to each one of the whole, not relative from one to another and from a third relative to a fourth. So their intention is not the permissibility of the single power attaching to this movement and that movement and to this rest and that rest also, with the existence of similarity between the two movements and the two rests.

His saying: ('and its abandonment') is an explicative conjunction for 'non-existence of the act', indicating that what is meant by it is not the eternal non-existence of the act but its abandonment, whether abandonment is interpreted as refraining the soul or as intentional non-existence of the act.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force'): Know that from the brain and spinal cord a nerve grows, and from the bone which is intended for movement a ligament. When the nerve and ligament meet, they form into fine shreds, and the gaps between them are filled with another flesh, and it is interwoven with the membrane, and that composite body of nerve and ligament is called the red body, and the membrane is a muscle. Then what emerges from the nerve and ligament transfers and becomes one body in sensation, intermediate between the softness of the nerve and the hardness of the ligament, and is called the tendon. Its benefit is that it bends at the knot of the muscle, so the arm intended for movement contracts, and it relaxes upon extension returning to its first description, or it extends from it upon its measure in its length when it is in the desired position of the limb.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force') – He said in the investigation of voiced letters from Sharh al-Mulakhkhas: The nerve is a body whose origin is the brain and spinal cord, and it is white, soft in bending, hard in separating; it was created to give the limbs sensation and movement. The ligament is a body similar to the nerve. When you know the nerve and ligament, know that the muscle is a composite of nerve and ligament and their fibers, and of the stuffing flesh in its interstices and the membrane covering it. When it hardens, the tendon occurs, which is the fusion of the ligament and the nerve extending from it to the side of the limb, and it contracts, thereby pulling the limb; and when it expands, the tendon relaxes so the limb moves away.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion on whether a single power (qudra) can relate to two opposite actions. The author begins by stating the topic: 'Power with an act does not relate to opposites.' He then presents several opinions. First, he reports a view distinguishing between heart-power and limb-power: heart-power relates to all its objects (beliefs, volitions), while limb-power relates only to some physical objects like movements. A second view says each power relates to all its own objects exclusively. A third view says each power relates to all objects of both categories but cannot effect the other's objects due to lacking the proper instrument (āla). A fourth view says heart-power relates to both categories whereas organic (limb-)power relates only to physical acts. Then Ibn al-Rawandi and many Ash'ari scholars hold that created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. The Mu'tazila unanimously agree that a single power relates to similar things (mutamāthilāt) of one type over successive times, and they all agree that one power cannot produce two similar acts at the same time. They claim as a necessity (ḍarūra) that power is essentially ability to perform both ends of an act; otherwise the agent would be compelled (muḍṭarr). The author refutes this by noting that the entire religious law—call to truth, reward, and punishment—is built on the assumption that the obligated person is genuinely capable. Then follows an answer: if by 'compelled' they mean the act is impossible for him, that is denied; if they mean his power is restricted to one specific act (not two opposites), we accept that, and it does not negate power. He gives an example: a man surrounded by walls is capable of staying in his place even though he cannot leave. Al-Āmidī argues that even if we concede that power must relate to both opposites, it could be multiple powers, not one. Al-Rāzī distinguishes two meanings of power: (1) pure force (quwwa) that is a principle for various animal actions and is equally related to opposites before the act, and (2) the force that has all conditions for effecting an action. The passage also includes detailed glosses explaining key terms: 'beliefs' means theoretical possibles; 'volitions' means possibles chosen; 'from one kind' specifies that similarity is relative to each individual, not cross-category. A note mentions that the Mu'tazila have various answers to the objection of forgetfulness, but they are not mentioned here. The anatomical commentary at the end explains the physical basis of muscular power, describing nerves, ligaments, muscles, and tendons, with their roles in movement.

Key Terms
قوة (quwwa)
قدرة (qudra)
شرائط التأثير (sharā'iṭ al-ta'thīr)
عضلية (ʿuḍaliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, which sense of power is equally related to opposites before the act?
A) Power as complete capacity (qudra).
B) Power as mere force (quwwa).
C) Both senses.
D) Neither sense.

Key Takeaway
The debate about power's scope is clarified by distinguishing between the principle of movement and the fully conditioned ability to act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice between two opposite actions, do you think of your power as a neutral force that you direct, or as something fully conditioned? How does that affect your sense of responsibility?
?
Al-Amidi suggests that multiple powers could be at work in you. Does it change how you see your own internal struggle when you face a moral dilemma? Will you now identify different 'powers' within yourself?
?
If your ability to act depends on gathering all conditions, what condition in your life are you neglecting right now that prevents you from doing what you know you should? What will you do about it today?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Anatomy of Muscular Power
The commentator explains the anatomical basis of muscular power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical composition of muscles and tendons as the basis for the term 'muscular power' (quwwa ʿuḍaliyya).

Core Idea
Muscular power depends on a composite of nerve, ligament, and flesh.

This passage provides a detailed anatomical description of the muscle and tendon system to explain what is meant by 'muscular power' used in the theological discussion. It shows that the power of the limbs is rooted in a specific physical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the description and identify the components
Look for the terms: nerve (عصب), ligament (رباط), muscle (عضلة), tendon (وتر), and flesh (لحم). Understand how they are arranged.
The muscle is a composite of nerve, ligament, and stuffing flesh.
2
Understand the function of the tendon
The tendon connects the muscle to the limb and, by contracting and relaxing, moves the limb.
The example of the arm illustrates how the tendon bends and extends.
3
Connect to the theological discussion
Reflect on why the author includes this: to ground the concept of 'muscular power' in observable biology, which underlies the distinction between heart and limb power.

العلم بطريان السهو، وللمعتزلة عن هذا الإلزام اختلافات مذكورة في أبكار الأفكار لا يناسب ذكرها في هذا المختصر.
قوله: ( من جنس واحد من المقدورات على تعاقب الأزمنة أي الموجودة على التعاقب والمراد بالجنس النوع فإن الجنس يطلق لغة على النوع كما أشار إليه الشارح في ثالث مباحث الحرارة فإن قلت المتماثلات لا تكون إلا من جنس واحد إذ التماثل كما سبق هو الاتحاد في النوع فما فائدة قول الشارح من جنس واحد؟ قلت: فائدته هي الإشارة إلى أن تماثل المتماثلات التي جوز المعتزلة تعلق القدرة بها بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من الجميع لا بالنسبة من واحد إلى آخر ومن ثالث بالنسبة إلى رابع فليس مرادهم جواز تعلق القدرة الواحدة بهذه الحركة وتلك الحركة وبهذا السكون ذلك السكون أيضاً مع وجود التماثل بين الحركتين والسكونين.

قوله: ( وتركه ) عطف تفسيري لعدم الفعل إشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد منه عدم الفعل الأزلي بل تركه سواء فسر الترك بكف النفس أو بعدم الفعل قصداً.

قوله: (وهي القوة الخ) اعلم أنه نبت من الدماغ والنخاع عصب ومن العظم الذي يراد حركة رباط، فإذا التقى العصب والرباط تشيا شظايا دقاقاً، ويحشى الفرج الواقعة بينهما لحماً آخر ويخلل الغشاء، ويسمى ذلك الجسم المركب من العصب والرباط الجسم الأحمر، والغشاء عضلة ثم ما يبرز من العصب والرباط ينتقل فيصير جسماً واحداً في الحس متوسطاً بين لين العصب، وصلابة الرباط ويسمى الوتر وفائدتها أن يتحدب عند عقاص العضلة فينقبض العضد المراد تحريك وليترخى عند انبساط عائدة إلى وصفها الأولى، أو زائدة منه على مقدارها في طولها حال كونها على وضع المطلوب فيه العضو.

قوله: ( وهي القوة العضلية ) قال في بحث الحروف المصوتة من شرح الملخص: العصب جسم منبته الدماغ والنخاع وهو أبيض لين في الانعطاف صلب في الانفصال خلق ليتم به للأعضاء الحس والحركة والرباط جسم شبيه بالعصب، وإذا عرفت العصب والرباط فاعلم أن العضلة جرم مؤلف من العصب والرباط ومن ليفهما، ومن اللحم الحاشي في خللها والعشاء لها، إذا تصلب حدث الوتر وهو الملتئم من الرباط والعصب النافذ منها إلى جانب العضو وهي فيتشنج فتجذب العضو، وإذا انبسطت استرخى الوتر فتباعد العضو.
Chapter Four - Seventh Aim: Power with an act does not relate to opposites. The power residing in the heart relates to all its objects such as beliefs and volitions and the like, but not the power residing in the limbs, for it does not relate to all of its possibles from intentions and movements and others. And he said another time: Each one of them relates to all its objects without the objects of the other. And he said a third time: Each one of them relates to its objects which are the actions of the hearts and limbs all together, except that each of them does not effect the objects of the other due to the lack of the instrument, meaning it is impossible to produce the actions of the limbs by the power residing in the heart due to the lack of instruments and the specific structure suitable for those actions, and likewise the opposite. And he said a fourth time: The heart's power relates to both of their objects together, but not the organic power, for it relates to the actions of the limbs without the actions of the hearts. And Ibn al-Rawandi from the Mu'tazila and many of our companions said: The created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. And the Mu'tazila unanimously agreed that it, i.e., the single power, relates to similar things of one genus from the possibles over successive times and seasons, with all of them agreeing that no two similar acts occur by it, i.e., that single power, in one place at one time, and that they, i.e., the Mu'tazila, claim in what they held, i.e., the relation of power to opposites, necessity, for there is no meaning to power except capability of the two ends, i.e., the two ends of the possible act, and whoever is not capable of the non-existence of the act and its abandonment which is its opposite and contradictory is compelled and forced to the act such that he cannot separate from it, not capable of it, and it is false. How? And upon it, i.e., upon the obligated being powerful and capable of the act, the call to the religion of truth and reward and punishment for the actions of the hearts and bodies is built. And if its relation to opposites is established, then its relation to non-opposites is more primary. And it was answered about that that if by 'compelled' it is meant that his act is not possible for him, then it is denied; and if by it is meant that his possible and the object of his power is specified and that he has no possible by this power other than it, then this is what we claim and adhere to, and there is no dispute for us in calling him compelled, for compulsion in the sense of impossibility of separation does not contradict power. Do you not see that one who is surrounded by a building from all his sides such that he is incapable of moving from one direction to another, is capable of his location by consensus from us and from them, and he has no way to separate from his possible. Al-Amidi said: And even if we concede that the one who is powerful over a thing must be powerful over its opposite, we say it is possible that the power related to them is multiple, not one. Imam al-Razi said: Power is applied to mere force which is a principle for the various animal actions, and it is the muscular power which is such that when the will for one of the two opposites is joined to it, that opposite occurs, and when the will for the other opposite is joined to it, that other occurs, and there is no doubt that its relation, i.e., the relation of this force, to the two opposites is equal, and it is before the act. And power is also applied to the force that has gathered all the conditions for effect, without doubt.

His saying: ('such as beliefs') which are theoretical and possible.
His saying: ('and also volitions') which are possible and others from the attribution of acceptance occurring by choice.
His saying: ('from one kind' i.e., one species, because similar things are of two species, like similar movements and similar rest.
Knowledge of the occurrence of forgetfulness, and for the Mu'tazila regarding this objection there are differences mentioned in Abkar al-Afkar, and it is not appropriate to mention them in this abridged work.
His saying: ('from one kind of the possibles over successive times') i.e., existing successively, and by 'kind' is meant 'species', for 'kind' is applied linguistically to 'species', as the commentator indicated in the third investigation of heat. For if you say: Similar things are only from one species, since similarity, as previously mentioned, is unity in species, then what is the benefit of the commentator's saying 'from one kind'? I say: Its benefit is to indicate that the similarity of the similar things which the Mu'tazila allowed the attachment of power to, is relative to each one of the whole, not relative from one to another and from a third relative to a fourth. So their intention is not the permissibility of the single power attaching to this movement and that movement and to this rest and that rest also, with the existence of similarity between the two movements and the two rests.

His saying: ('and its abandonment') is an explicative conjunction for 'non-existence of the act', indicating that what is meant by it is not the eternal non-existence of the act but its abandonment, whether abandonment is interpreted as refraining the soul or as intentional non-existence of the act.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force'): Know that from the brain and spinal cord a nerve grows, and from the bone which is intended for movement a ligament. When the nerve and ligament meet, they form into fine shreds, and the gaps between them are filled with another flesh, and it is interwoven with the membrane, and that composite body of nerve and ligament is called the red body, and the membrane is a muscle. Then what emerges from the nerve and ligament transfers and becomes one body in sensation, intermediate between the softness of the nerve and the hardness of the ligament, and is called the tendon. Its benefit is that it bends at the knot of the muscle, so the arm intended for movement contracts, and it relaxes upon extension returning to its first description, or it extends from it upon its measure in its length when it is in the desired position of the limb.

His saying: ('and it is the muscular force') – He said in the investigation of voiced letters from Sharh al-Mulakhkhas: The nerve is a body whose origin is the brain and spinal cord, and it is white, soft in bending, hard in separating; it was created to give the limbs sensation and movement. The ligament is a body similar to the nerve. When you know the nerve and ligament, know that the muscle is a composite of nerve and ligament and their fibers, and of the stuffing flesh in its interstices and the membrane covering it. When it hardens, the tendon occurs, which is the fusion of the ligament and the nerve extending from it to the side of the limb, and it contracts, thereby pulling the limb; and when it expands, the tendon relaxes so the limb moves away.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion on whether a single power (qudra) can relate to two opposite actions. The author begins by stating the topic: 'Power with an act does not relate to opposites.' He then presents several opinions. First, he reports a view distinguishing between heart-power and limb-power: heart-power relates to all its objects (beliefs, volitions), while limb-power relates only to some physical objects like movements. A second view says each power relates to all its own objects exclusively. A third view says each power relates to all objects of both categories but cannot effect the other's objects due to lacking the proper instrument (āla). A fourth view says heart-power relates to both categories whereas organic (limb-)power relates only to physical acts. Then Ibn al-Rawandi and many Ash'ari scholars hold that created power relates to opposites alternately, not simultaneously. The Mu'tazila unanimously agree that a single power relates to similar things (mutamāthilāt) of one type over successive times, and they all agree that one power cannot produce two similar acts at the same time. They claim as a necessity (ḍarūra) that power is essentially ability to perform both ends of an act; otherwise the agent would be compelled (muḍṭarr). The author refutes this by noting that the entire religious law—call to truth, reward, and punishment—is built on the assumption that the obligated person is genuinely capable. Then follows an answer: if by 'compelled' they mean the act is impossible for him, that is denied; if they mean his power is restricted to one specific act (not two opposites), we accept that, and it does not negate power. He gives an example: a man surrounded by walls is capable of staying in his place even though he cannot leave. Al-Āmidī argues that even if we concede that power must relate to both opposites, it could be multiple powers, not one. Al-Rāzī distinguishes two meanings of power: (1) pure force (quwwa) that is a principle for various animal actions and is equally related to opposites before the act, and (2) the force that has all conditions for effecting an action. The passage also includes detailed glosses explaining key terms: 'beliefs' means theoretical possibles; 'volitions' means possibles chosen; 'from one kind' specifies that similarity is relative to each individual, not cross-category. A note mentions that the Mu'tazila have various answers to the objection of forgetfulness, but they are not mentioned here. The anatomical commentary at the end explains the physical basis of muscular power, describing nerves, ligaments, muscles, and tendons, with their roles in movement.

رباط (ribāṭ)
عضلة (ʿaḍala)
وتر (watar)
لحم (laḥm)

Key Takeaway
The physical structure of muscles and tendons enables the limb movements that are objects of muscular power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every time you move a limb, a precise sequence of nerve signals, ligament tension, muscle contraction, and tendon pull occurs. When you are about to use your body for something displeasing to Allah, does the thought of this intricate gift make you hesitate? Will you consciously pause today to thank the One who designed you so carefully, before you act?
?
This passage reveals that your very ability to stretch out your hand for prayer or stand in humility depends on a composite of flesh, nerve, and ligament. How does knowing this change the way you value your physical strength? Will you dedicate even one small movement today to an act of worship, as a recognition that every motion is a trust from your Creator?

The Problem of Sleep and Ability: Initial Question and Agreement
The author poses the problem and states the initial agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core problem of whether sleep negates ability and the initial agreement that many precise actions are impossible for a sleeper but a few are possible.

Core Idea
Sleep does not completely remove ability; a few precise actions may remain within the sleeper's power.

This passage begins a deep theological discussion on the nature of ability (qudra) during sleep. The author first poses a fundamental question: does sleep oppose ability, making the sleeper incapable of deliberate action? He then reports an initial agreement between the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris that while many precise actions cannot occur from a sleeper, a few such actions are possible based on experience. This lesson lays the groundwork for understanding the nuanced positions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author frames the problem as a direct question.
The opening question sets the stage for the entire discussion.
2
Identify the initial agreement
Find the sentence where the author says 'agreed upon the impossibility of many precise actions and the possibility of a few'. This is the baseline all parties accept.
3
Reflect on why 'experience' is mentioned
The agreement is based on 'experience' (tajriba). Think about what everyday observation tells us about sleeping people's actions.
Experience is a source of knowledge in this context.

The Tenth Topic: The Saying of the Philosophers about Sleep and Ability. Is sleep opposite to ability so that the action of the sleeper is not within his power, or is it not opposite to it, so that his action may be within his power? We say: The Mu'tazila and many of us have agreed upon the impossibility of the occurrence of many precise actions from the sleeper and the possibility of the occurrence of a few precise actions from him by experience. Then those who permitted differed concerning these few actions: it was said that they are within his power even if he has no knowledge of them. [Commentary:] His statement: 'it was said: they are within his power even if he has no knowledge of them' requires examination, because the capable one is the one who acts with intention and choice, and that cannot be conceived except with knowledge. Moreover, the inference from ability to knowledge is the ancient path of the verifiers among the theologians, as will come in the divine matters. What he permitted here of a few precise actions issuing from a capable one who is not knowledgeable is far-fetched because the aforementioned explanation encompasses both the few and the many. And it may be said in response: perhaps they had no knowledge of them, because sleep does not oppose ability while it opposes knowledge and other perceptions by agreement of the rational people. Al-ustadh Abu Ishaq said: they are not within his power, for sleep opposes ability just as it opposes knowledge and all perceptions. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and many of our companions suspended judgment and said: there is no certainty that those actions are acquired by the sleeper nor that they are necessary for him; rather, both are possible without preference. Al-Amidi said: we may claim necessity in the knowledge that they are within the power of the sleeper, insofar as we distinguish between the trembling of his hand in his sleep and his turning over, clenching his hand, and opening it, just as we distinguish between them in the case of the awake person without any difference. Whoever seeks to equate them in the sleeper is not far from skepticism in equating them in the case of the awake person, and that is far from reason. He said: this, although it is in
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the problem of whether sleep negates the ability to act. The author begins by stating the question: Is sleep opposite to ability, so that the sleeper's actions are not within his power, or not? He then reports the initial agreement between the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris that many expert, precise actions cannot come from a sleeper, but a few can, based on experience. Then he notes the disagreement among those who permit the few actions: some say they are within his power even without knowledge. The commentary then critiques this view: the capable one acts with intention and choice, which require knowledge. The inference from ability to knowledge is a classical theological method. Permitting a few precise actions from an ignorant capable agent is problematic because the reasoning applies to both few and many. A possible response is offered: perhaps they meant sleep does not oppose ability, only knowledge, by consensus. Then three further opinions are given: al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq holds that sleep does oppose ability; al-Qadi Abu Bakr and many suspend judgment; and al-Amidi claims a necessary distinction between involuntary and voluntary movements, insisting that some actions of the sleeper are clearly within his power.

القدرة
المقدور
الكسب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sleep completely eliminates the ability to act deliberately.
Correction: The passage shows an initial agreement that many precise actions are impossible, but a few may be possible, indicating that sleep does not altogether abolish ability.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the initial agreement between the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris regarding the actions of a sleeper?
A) All actions of a sleeper are impossible.
B) Many precise actions are impossible, but a few are possible by experience.
C) All actions of a sleeper are within his power.
D) Only involuntary actions are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The initial agreement establishes that while many deliberate actions are impossible during sleep, a few such actions can still occur based on empirical observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the sleeper is not fully responsible for many actions, yet still accountable for some, how does that change your view of Allah's justice? Write one specific area where you need to take more conscious control.
?
The scholars here rely on experience (tajriba) to settle a theological question. When faced with uncertainty about your faith, are you willing to consult both revelation and your own conscious experience? What step will you take today to test a belief against your life experience?

Critique of the 'Power without Knowledge' View
The author reports and then critiques the view that the sleeper's few actions are within his power even without knowledge, with a possible response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique against the claim that a sleeper can have power over actions without possessing knowledge of them, and the possible response that sleep does not oppose ability.

Core Idea
True ability requires intention and choice, which depend on knowledge.

After stating the initial agreement, the author turns to a specific view: some thinkers permit a few precise actions from the sleeper and claim these are within his power even without knowledge. The commentary launches a sharp critique, arguing that capability (qudra) intrinsically requires knowledge (ʿilm), pointing to a classical theological principle that infers knowledge from ability. This lesson unpacks that critique and the attempted defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic from 'Then those who permitted differed...' up to 'by agreement of the rational people'. Follow both the statement of the view and its critique.
2
Identify the key claim being critiqued
The view is: the few precise actions are within the sleeper's power even if he has no knowledge of them. Mark this.
This view is being examined.
3
Understand the main critique
The critique says that a capable agent (qādir) acts with intention and choice (qaṣd wa-ikhtiyār), which cannot be conceived without knowledge. Also, from ability one can infer knowledge—a classical method.
This is the core argument.
4
Examine the response
A possible defense is offered: perhaps the sleeper had no knowledge because sleep opposes knowledge but not ability, according to consensus.
The response challenges the premise that sleep must also oppose ability.

المقصد العاشر : قول الحكماء حول النوم والقدرة . هل النوم ضد للقدرة فلا يكون حينئذ فعل النائم مقدوراً له أو ليس ضداً لها، فجاز أن يكون فعله مقدوراً له؟ فنقول : اتفقت المعتزلة وكثير منا على امتناع صدور الأفعال المتقنة الكثيرة من النائم وجواز صدور الأفعال المحكمة القليلة منه بالتجربة ثم اختلف المجوزون في هذه الأفعال القليلة فقيل : هي مقدورة له وإن كان لا علم له بها . قوله : ( فقيل : هي مقدورة له وإن كان لا علم له بها فيه بحث لأن القادر هو الذي يفعل بالقصد والاختيار، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بالعلم كيف والاستدلال بالقدرة على العلم هو المسلك القديم عند محققي المتكلمين كما سيجيء في الإلهيات، وما جوزه هاهنا من صدور قليل متقن عن قادر غير عالم فبعيد لأن البيان المذكور يعم القليل والكثير، وقد يقال في الجواب : لعلهم كان لا علم له بها فإن النوم لا يضاد القدرة مع كونه مضاداً للعلم وغيره من الإدراكات باتفاق العقلاء وقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : هي غير مقدورة له فإن النوم يضاد القدرة كما يضاد العلم وسائر الإدراكات وتوقف القاضي أبو بكر وكثير من أصحابنا وقالوا : لا قطع بكون تلك الأفعال مكتسبة للنائم ولا بكونها ضرورية له، بل كلاهما محتمل بلا ترجيح قال الآمدي : قد ندعي الضرورة في العلم بكونها مقدورة للنائم من حيث أنا نفرق بين ارتعاد يده في نومه وبين تقلبه وقبض يده وبسطها، كما نفرق بينهما في حق المستيقظ من غير فرق، ومن رام التسوية بينهما في النائم لم يبعد عنه التشكيك في تسويتهما في حق اليقظان وهو بعيد عن المعقول قال : هذا وإن كان في
The Tenth Topic: The Saying of the Philosophers about Sleep and Ability. Is sleep opposite to ability so that the action of the sleeper is not within his power, or is it not opposite to it, so that his action may be within his power? We say: The Mu'tazila and many of us have agreed upon the impossibility of the occurrence of many precise actions from the sleeper and the possibility of the occurrence of a few precise actions from him by experience. Then those who permitted differed concerning these few actions: it was said that they are within his power even if he has no knowledge of them. [Commentary:] His statement: 'it was said: they are within his power even if he has no knowledge of them' requires examination, because the capable one is the one who acts with intention and choice, and that cannot be conceived except with knowledge. Moreover, the inference from ability to knowledge is the ancient path of the verifiers among the theologians, as will come in the divine matters. What he permitted here of a few precise actions issuing from a capable one who is not knowledgeable is far-fetched because the aforementioned explanation encompasses both the few and the many. And it may be said in response: perhaps they had no knowledge of them, because sleep does not oppose ability while it opposes knowledge and other perceptions by agreement of the rational people. Al-ustadh Abu Ishaq said: they are not within his power, for sleep opposes ability just as it opposes knowledge and all perceptions. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and many of our companions suspended judgment and said: there is no certainty that those actions are acquired by the sleeper nor that they are necessary for him; rather, both are possible without preference. Al-Amidi said: we may claim necessity in the knowledge that they are within the power of the sleeper, insofar as we distinguish between the trembling of his hand in his sleep and his turning over, clenching his hand, and opening it, just as we distinguish between them in the case of the awake person without any difference. Whoever seeks to equate them in the sleeper is not far from skepticism in equating them in the case of the awake person, and that is far from reason. He said: this, although it is in
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the problem of whether sleep negates the ability to act. The author begins by stating the question: Is sleep opposite to ability, so that the sleeper's actions are not within his power, or not? He then reports the initial agreement between the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris that many expert, precise actions cannot come from a sleeper, but a few can, based on experience. Then he notes the disagreement among those who permit the few actions: some say they are within his power even without knowledge. The commentary then critiques this view: the capable one acts with intention and choice, which require knowledge. The inference from ability to knowledge is a classical theological method. Permitting a few precise actions from an ignorant capable agent is problematic because the reasoning applies to both few and many. A possible response is offered: perhaps they meant sleep does not oppose ability, only knowledge, by consensus. Then three further opinions are given: al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq holds that sleep does oppose ability; al-Qadi Abu Bakr and many suspend judgment; and al-Amidi claims a necessary distinction between involuntary and voluntary movements, insisting that some actions of the sleeper are clearly within his power.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A sleeper can perform deliberate actions without any knowledge.
Correction: The critique argues that true capability requires intention and choice, which necessitate knowledge; thus power without knowledge is problematic.

Checkpoint
What is the main critique against the view that a sleeper can have power over actions without knowledge?
A) Sleep completely negates ability.
B) The capable one must act with intention and choice, which require knowledge.
C) Experience shows that no actions occur from a sleeper.
D) The Mu'tazila rejected this view.

Key Takeaway
The ability to act deliberately (qudra) is intrinsically linked to knowledge (ʿilm); the notion of power without knowledge is critically examined and defended only by arguing that sleep does not oppose ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted without true knowledge—perhaps speaking on a topic you did not fully understand? How does this passage call you to examine whether those actions were truly within your power or merely impulsive?
?
If intention and choice require knowledge, then every decision you make is a product of what you know. Are you deliberately seeking knowledge to improve the quality of your choices, or are you content to act ignorantly?
?
This lesson teaches that ability without knowledge is problematic. In your relationship with your Creator, do you presume to act on your own ability without seeking the knowledge of what pleases Him? What step will you take today to ground your actions in knowledge?

Three Opinions on the Sleeper's Actions: Abu Ishaq, Qadi Abu Bakr, and al-Amidi
The author presents three final opinions on whether the sleeper's actions are within his power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three distinct theological positions regarding whether a sleeper's actions are within his power: opposition (Abu Ishaq), suspension (Qadi Abu Bakr), and distinction (al-Amidi).

Core Idea
Theologians disagree on whether sleep negates ability; some say yes, some suspend judgment, and some distinguish between voluntary and involuntary movements.

After the critique of the 'power without knowledge' view, the author presents three more authoritative positions. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq holds that sleep opposes ability completely. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and many Ash'aris suspend judgment, seeing no decisive evidence either way. Finally, al-Amidi argues for a necessary distinction: involuntary tremors are not within the sleeper's power, but deliberate movements like turning over clearly are. This lesson surveys these opinions and their rationales.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'Al-ustadh Abu Ishaq said...' to the end
Read the Arabic and translation for the three opinions. Each opinion is introduced by the scholar's name.
2
Identify each opinion and its reasoning
For Abu Ishaq: sleep opposes ability. For Qadi Abu Bakr: no certainty, both possibilities are open. For al-Amidi: we necessarily know the sleeper has power over some actions based on distinguishing voluntary from involuntary movements.
3
Compare the three positions
Notice how they range from definitive negation to definitive affirmation, with a middle position of suspension.
This reflects the spectrum of theological reasoning on the issue.
4
Analyze al-Amidi's argument in detail
Al-Amidi uses a phenomenological argument: we directly perceive a difference between a trembling hand and a turning over in sleep, just as in wakefulness. This difference grounds the judgment of power.
This is an appeal to immediate knowledge (ḍarūra).

The Tenth Topic: The Saying of the Philosophers about Sleep and Ability. Is sleep opposite to ability so that the action of the sleeper is not within his power, or is it not opposite to it, so that his action may be within his power? We say: The Mu'tazila and many of us have agreed upon the impossibility of the occurrence of many precise actions from the sleeper and the possibility of the occurrence of a few precise actions from him by experience. Then those who permitted differed concerning these few actions: it was said that they are within his power even if he has no knowledge of them. [Commentary:] His statement: 'it was said: they are within his power even if he has no knowledge of them' requires examination, because the capable one is the one who acts with intention and choice, and that cannot be conceived except with knowledge. What he permitted here of a few precise actions issuing from a capable one who is not knowledgeable is far-fetched because the aforementioned explanation encompasses both the few and the many. What would it cost you to admit that they are within your power?

Ability Does Not Require Detailed Knowledge
Author states will and ability are not tied to knowledge in all actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ability and will are not dependent on exhaustive detailed knowledge of every aspect of an action, but only on general intention.

Core Idea
Ability and will need intention, not every detail.

This passage begins a discussion on the relationship between ability, will, and knowledge. The author challenges the assumption that for an action to be within one's ability or will, one must know every minute detail of it. He clarifies that only actions proceeding from intention require some knowledge, but not exhaustive detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic: 'قوله : ( وإن كان لا علم له بها والإرادة والقدرة ليست تابعة للعلم والإرادة في جميع الأفعال، بل الصادرة عن قصد كما عرفت'. Understand that ability and will are not tied to knowledge and will in all actions, only those from intention.
The phrase 'كما عرفت' refers to previous knowledge about intentional actions.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the author distinguishes between actions that proceed from intention (قصد) and others. Ability and will are only relevant to intentional actions, not every action.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it prevents the misconception that God's ability requires Him to know every detail of all possible actions. General knowledge suffices.
This connects to the broader theology of divine attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن كان لا علم له بها والإرادة والقدرة ليست تابعة للعلم والإرادة في جميع الأفعال، بل الصادرة عن قصد كما عرفت وما قيل من أنهم لو استدلوا على ثبوت علمه تعالى بأنه قادر وكل قادر عالم، لأن القادر ما يكون فعله من قصد وإرادة، ولا يتصور ذلك بدون العلم، فإنما يدل على أن كل قادر لا بد أن يكون عالماً مريداً، لا أن كل ما يتعلق به القدرة يجب أن يكون معلوماً ومراداً، ولم يثبت بهذا الدليل عموم علمه تعالى فتدبر فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام . قوله : ( غير مقدورة له بل هو صادرة عن الطبيعة بحسب عروض العوارض وإتقانها اتفاقي أن مكتسبة والأولى مقدورة، لأن الكسب تعلق القدرة على وفق الإرادة ولا إرادة فيها ولا شعور . قوله: (لم يبعد عن التشكيك في تسويتهما) أي التشكيك في الفرق تجويز ثبوتها فالكلام على حذف وكلمة في التعليل. يقولون بقيام الشعور بجزء وقيام النوم بجزء آخر، كما يقوله الأستاذ، وفيه نظر لأن المفروض أن القدرة قائمة بما يقوم به النوم كما يشهد به السياق والمدعى أن الذي يقوم به القدرة لا بد أن يقوم به العلم بمقدوره الصادر عنه فليتأمل . قوله : ( وقال الأستاذ أبو سحاق هي غير مقدورة له أي الأفعال الخارجية غير مقدورة له، فلا ينافي ما اختاره بعيد هذا من عدم الفرق بين إدراك النائم وإدراك اليقظان فيكون إدراك النائم أيضاً مقدوراً كإدراك اليقظان، والأظهر في دفع المنافاة أن مراده من عدم الفرق بينهما عدمه في الإدراكية لا عدمه من جميع الوجوه حتى يشمل المقدورية وعدمها . قوله : (لم يبعد عنه التشكيك في تسويتهما في حق اليقظان أراد التسوية في الضرورية وعدم المقدورية، كما أنه المراد بالتسوية بينهما في النائم، ثم الأظهر أن متعلق التشكيك المدعى بعده عن المعقول هو الفرق بينهما الثابت بلا شبهة لانفس التسوية، لأن المتبادر حينئذ أن التسوية ثابتة في نفس الأمر، فالتشكيك فيها بعيد عن المعقول، وليس المراد هذا فكان مراده التشكيك الثابت في تسويتهما فإن التسوية متضمنة للتشكيك في الفرق ويمكن أن يحمل على ظاهره ويكون بعده عن المعقول بناء على أن إيقاع الشك في التسوية يتضمن تجويزها لأن الشك تساوي الطرفين فليفهم .
His statement: 'Even if he has no knowledge of them'—will and ability are not dependent on knowledge and will in all actions, but rather on those proceeding from intention, as you have known. And what was said that they inferred the existence of His knowledge—exalted is He—from His being capable and every capable one is knowledgeable, because the capable one is one whose action is from intention and will, and that cannot be conceived without knowledge, only indicates that every capable one must be knowing and willing, not that everything related to ability must be known and willed. By this argument, the universality of His knowledge—exalted is He—has not been established. So reflect, for many feet have slipped there. His statement: 'not within his power'—rather it proceeds from nature according to the occurrence of accidents, and its precision is accidental. That it is acquired and the first is within power, because acquisition is the attachment of ability in accordance with will, and there is no will and no awareness in it. His statement: 'he is not far from skepticism in equating them'—meaning skepticism in the difference, permitting its existence; so the speech is elliptical and the word is in the explanation. They say: by the existence of perception in one part and the existence of sleep in another part, as the teacher says. But there is consideration because it is assumed that ability subsists in what sleep subsists in, as the context indicates, and the claim is that that in which ability subsists must have knowledge of the action issuing from it. So reflect. His statement: 'And al-ustadh Abu Ishaq said: they are not within his power'—meaning external actions are not within his power, so it does not contradict what he chose far from this regarding the non-difference between the sleeper's perception and the awake person's perception; thus the sleeper's perception is also within power like the awake person's perception. The more apparent in repelling the contradiction is that his meaning of no difference between them is no difference in perceptiveness, not no difference in all respects to include power and lack thereof. His statement: 'he is not far from skepticism in equating them in the case of the awake person'—he intended the equation in necessity and lack of power, as is the intention in equating them in the sleeper. Then the more apparent is that the object of the skepticism claimed to be far from reason is the difference between them established without doubt, not the equation itself, because the immediate understanding then is that the equation is established in reality, so skepticism about it is far from reason, and this is not the intention. Rather, his intention was the skepticism established in equating them, for the equation entails skepticism about the difference. It can be taken at face value, and its being far from reason is based on the fact that casting doubt on the equation entails permitting it, because doubt is equality of the two sides. So understand.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the implications of the claim that the sleeper's actions are within his power even without knowledge. The author explains that will and ability are not necessarily tied to detailed knowledge of the entire action; they are tied to intentional actions. He then critiques a common theological argument: inferring God's knowledge from His ability does not prove that all objects of ability are known and willed in detail. Rather, it only proves that every capable being must be knowing and willing in general. He then discusses the distinction between actions of nature and actions of choice, and clarifies that acquisition (kasb) is the attachment of ability according to will, which requires no awareness. The commentary also resolves a supposed contradiction in al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's position: he denies ability for external actions but affirms it for perception because perception is not an external action. Finally, the author explains the meaning of skepticism in equating involuntary and voluntary movements: equating them would imply that there is no difference, which is false; but the skeptic's intent might be to cast doubt on the difference itself, which is unreasonable because the difference is evident.

القدرة
العلم التفصيلي
القصد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being capable of an action requires knowing every detail of it.
Correction: The passage states that ability and will are not dependent on exhaustive knowledge; only intentional actions require some knowledge, but not necessarily detailed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do ability and will depend on?
A) Exhaustive knowledge of all action details
B) Actions proceeding from intention
C) All actions equally
D) The absence of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Ability and will are not tied to exhaustive knowledge; they relate only to intentional actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Argument from Ability Does Not Prove Universal Knowledge
Author critiques the inference from ability to universal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theological argument that God's ability proves His universal knowledge of all things is insufficient to establish that every object of ability is known in detail.

Core Idea
Ability proves knowledge and will, not that everything is known.

Some theologians argued that since God is capable (qadir) and every capable being must be knowledgeable, God must know all that He is able to do. The author critiques this reasoning, showing that it only proves that God is knowing and willing in general, not that every particular thing related to His ability is known and willed in detail. This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Examine the argument presented
Read: 'وما قيل من أنهم لو استدلوا على ثبوت علمه تعالى بأنه قادر وكل قادر عالم، لأن القادر ما يكون فعله من قصد وإرادة، ولا يتصور ذلك بدون العلم'. Understand the inference: God is capable → every capable being is knowledgeable → God knows.
The argument seems plausible at first glance.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author says: 'فإنما يدل على أن كل قادر لا بد أن يكون عالماً مريداً، لا أن كل ما يتعلق به القدرة يجب أن يكون معلوماً ومراداً'. He distinguishes between the agent's attributes and the objects of his ability.
The argument only proves the agent has knowledge and will, not that everything He can do is known in detail.
3
Appreciate the consequence
The author warns: 'ولم يثبت بهذا الدليل عموم علمه تعالى فتدبر فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام'. This is a subtle point where many have slipped.
The universality of God's knowledge is not proven by this argument alone.

قوله : ( وإن كان لا علم له بها والإرادة والقدرة ليست تابعة للعلم والإرادة في جميع الأفعال، بل الصادرة عن قصد كما عرفت وما قيل من أنهم لو استدلوا على ثبوت علمه تعالى بأنه قادر وكل قادر عالم، لأن القادر ما يكون فعله من قصد وإرادة، ولا يتصور ذلك بدون العلم، فإنما يدل على أن كل قادر لا بد أن يكون عالماً مريداً، لا أن كل ما يتعلق به القدرة يجب أن يكون معلوماً ومراداً، ولم يثبت بهذا الدليل عموم علمه تعالى فتدبر فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام . قوله : ( غير مقدورة له بل هو صادرة عن الطبيعة بحسب عروض العوارض وإتقانها اتفاقي أن مكتسبة والأولى مقدورة، لأن الكسب تعلق القدرة على وفق الإرادة ولا إرادة فيها ولا شعور . قوله: (لم يبعد عن التشكيك في تسويتهما) أي التشكيك في الفرق تجويز ثبوتها فالكلام على حذف وكلمة في التعليل. يقولون بقيام الشعور بجزء وقيام النوم بجزء آخر، كما يقوله الأستاذ، وفيه نظر لأن المفروض أن القدرة قائمة بما يقوم به النوم كما يشهد به السياق والمدعى أن الذي يقوم به القدرة لا بد أن يقوم به العلم بمقدوره الصادر عنه فليتأمل . قوله : ( وقال الأستاذ أبو سحاق هي غير مقدورة له أي الأفعال الخارجية غير مقدورة له، فلا ينافي ما اختاره بعيد هذا من عدم الفرق بين إدراك النائم وإدراك اليقظان فيكون إدراك النائم أيضاً مقدوراً كإدراك اليقظان، والأظهر في دفع المنافاة أن مراده من عدم الفرق بينهما عدمه في الإدراكية لا عدمه من جميع الوجوه حتى يشمل المقدورية وعدمها . قوله : (لم يبعد عنه التشكيك في تسويتهما في حق اليقظان أراد التسوية في الضرورية وعدم المقدورية، كما أنه المراد بالتسوية بينهما في النائم، ثم الأظهر أن متعلق التشكيك المدعى بعده عن المعقول هو الفرق بينهما الثابت بلا شبهة لانفس التسوية، لأن المتبادر حينئذ أن التسوية ثابتة في نفس الأمر، فالتشكيك فيها بعيد عن المعقول، وليس المراد هذا فكان مراده التشكيك الثابت في تسويتهما فإن التسوية متضمنة للتشكيك في الفرق ويمكن أن يحمل على ظاهره ويكون بعده عن المعقول بناء على أن إيقاع الشك في التسوية يتضمن تجويزها لأن الشك تساوي الطرفين فليفهم .
His statement: 'Even if he has no knowledge of them'—will and ability are not dependent on knowledge and will in all actions, but rather on those proceeding from intention, as you have known. And what was said that they inferred the existence of His knowledge—exalted is He—from His being capable and every capable one is knowledgeable, because the capable one is one whose action is from intention and will, and that cannot be conceived without knowledge, only indicates that every capable one must be knowing and willing, not that everything related to ability must be known and willed. By this argument, the universality of His knowledge—exalted is He—has not been established. So reflect, for many feet have slipped there. His statement: 'not within his power'—rather it proceeds from nature according to the occurrence of accidents, and its precision is accidental. That it is acquired and the first is within power, because acquisition is the attachment of ability in accordance with will, and there is no will and no awareness in it. His statement: 'he is not far from skepticism in equating them'—meaning skepticism in the difference, permitting its existence; so the speech is elliptical and the word is in the explanation. They say: by the existence of perception in one part and the existence of sleep in another part, as the teacher says. But there is consideration because it is assumed that ability subsists in what sleep subsists in, as the context indicates, and the claim is that that in which ability subsists must have knowledge of the action issuing from it. So reflect. His statement: 'And al-ustadh Abu Ishaq said: they are not within his power'—meaning external actions are not within his power, so it does not contradict what he chose far from this regarding the non-difference between the sleeper's perception and the awake person's perception; thus the sleeper's perception is also within power like the awake person's perception. The more apparent in repelling the contradiction is that his meaning of no difference between them is no difference in perceptiveness, not no difference in all respects to include power and lack thereof. His statement: 'he is not far from skepticism in equating them in the case of the awake person'—he intended the equation in necessity and lack of power, as is the intention in equating them in the sleeper. Then the more apparent is that the object of the skepticism claimed to be far from reason is the difference between them established without doubt, not the equation itself, because the immediate understanding then is that the equation is established in reality, so skepticism about it is far from reason, and this is not the intention. Rather, his intention was the skepticism established in equating them, for the equation entails skepticism about the difference. It can be taken at face value, and its being far from reason is based on the fact that casting doubt on the equation entails permitting it, because doubt is equality of the two sides. So understand.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the implications of the claim that the sleeper's actions are within his power even without knowledge. The author explains that will and ability are not necessarily tied to detailed knowledge of the entire action; they are tied to intentional actions. He then critiques a common theological argument: inferring God's knowledge from His ability does not prove that all objects of ability are known and willed in detail. Rather, it only proves that every capable being must be knowing and willing in general. He then discusses the distinction between actions of nature and actions of choice, and clarifies that acquisition (kasb) is the attachment of ability according to will, which requires no awareness. The commentary also resolves a supposed contradiction in al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's position: he denies ability for external actions but affirms it for perception because perception is not an external action. Finally, the author explains the meaning of skepticism in equating involuntary and voluntary movements: equating them would imply that there is no difference, which is false; but the skeptic's intent might be to cast doubt on the difference itself, which is unreasonable because the difference is evident.

القدرة
القادر
العالم
العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument 'God is capable → God is knowledgeable' proves that God knows every single thing He can do in detail.
Correction: The author clarifies that it only proves God is knowing and willing in general, not that every object of ability is known.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the argument from ability actually proves?
A) That every object of God's ability is known in detail
B) That God is knowing and willing, but not that everything is known
C) That God does not need knowledge
D) That the argument is completely false

Key Takeaway
The argument from ability to divine knowledge only proves God's general knowledge and will, not detailed knowledge of every possible object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson warns that even scholars have stumbled by accepting a partial argument as complete. When you hold a belief about God's attributes, do you rely on a single proof, or have you investigated multiple lines of evidence to ensure your understanding is sound? Will you now examine your own theological reasoning more critically?
?
The author says the argument only proves that God has knowledge and will, not that He knows every detail of what He can do. Are you comfortable admitting that some aspects of God's attributes require multiple proofs, or do you feel unease when one argument seems insufficient? How does your trust in God's perfection coexist with the limits of human reasoning?
?
Many feet have slipped on this point. Reflect on a time when you were overconfident in a religious conclusion, only to later find a flaw in your reasoning. What did that experience teach you about humility before definitive claims? How can this lesson guard you against similar mistakes in your worship or understanding?

Acquisition and the Sleeper's Actions
Author defines kasb and discusses sleeper's actions and perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of acquisition (kasb) and how actions of the sleeper, though without awareness, can still be attributed to the agent through ability, distinguishing between voluntary and involuntary acts.

Core Idea
Acquisition is ability applied with will; sleepers act without awareness.

This lesson delves into the distinction between actions that are 'acquired' (maksubah) and those that occur by nature. The author defines acquisition as the attachment of ability in accordance with will, emphasizing that no awareness is required. He then addresses a potential contradiction regarding the sleeper's perception: though lacking will and awareness, it is still considered within the sleeper's power (as perception, not external action).

Guided Path
1
Define 'acquisition' (kasb)
Read: 'لأن الكسب تعلق القدرة على وفق الإرادة ولا إرادة فيها ولا شعور'. Acquisition is ability attached according to will, but the action itself may lack will and awareness.
This definition is from the Ash'ari tradition.
2
Distinguish natural from acquired actions
The author says: 'غير مقدورة له بل هو صادرة عن الطبيعة بحسب عروض العوارض وإتقانها اتفاقي أن مكتسبة والأولى مقدورة'. Some actions proceed from nature, not power, and are accidental; the first type (acquired) is within power.
Actions of nature (like growth) are not under our control.
3
Understand the sleeper's case
The author discusses Abu Ishaq's view: external actions are not within the sleeper's power, but perception is. 'فيكون إدراك النائم أيضاً مقدوراً كإدراك اليقظان'. So the sleeper's perception is within power, because it is not an external action.
This resolves the seeming contradiction.

?

?

?
This lesson teaches that perception itself is an action within your power. If you constantly fill your eyes and mind with what is forbidden, can you honestly say that is not a choice? What one specific change will you make today regarding what you allow yourself to perceive?

Three Positions on Actions During Sleep
al-Iji presents three positions on actions during sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on whether a sleeper's actions are within his power and the rational arguments behind each.

Core Idea
Actions during sleep spark a debate: are they within our power or not, and can we tell the difference?

This passage presents a key debate in kalam about human agency during sleep. Scholars disagree on whether a sleeper's actions—like turning over or grasping—are within his power. The discussion involves distinctions between voluntary and involuntary movements, and touches on the nature of power (qudra) itself. As you read, notice how each scholar uses a different criterion to reach their conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Iji lists the philosophers' view before the three positions.
2
Identify the three positions
Find the statements of al-Asfahani, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, and al-Amidi. Note how each is introduced and what argument they give.
Al-Asfahani denies power, al-Qadi Abu Bakr suspends judgment, al-Amidi affirms power based on differentiation.
3
Understand al-Amidi's argument
Al-Amidi uses the distinction between trembling and voluntary movements to argue that some actions in sleep are within power. Reflect on why this distinction is important.
He claims the same distinction holds for both awake and asleep.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each position affects our understanding of responsibility for actions during sleep. Which view seems most convincing to you?
This connects to broader issues of human agency and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد العاشر : قول الحكماء حول النوم والقدرة كان لا علم له بها فإن النوم لا يضاد القدرة مع كونه مضاداً للعلم وغيره من الإدراكات باتفاق العقلاء وقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : هي غير مقدورة له فإن النوم يضاد القدرة كما يضاد العلم وسائر الإدراكات وتوقف القاضي أبو بكر وكثير من أصحابنا وقالوا : لا قطع بكون تلك الأفعال مكتسبة للنائم ولا بكونها ضرورية له، بل كلاهما محتمل بلا ترجيح قال الآمدي : قد ندعي الضرورة في العلم بكونها مقدورة للنائم من حيث أنا نفرق بين ارتعاد يده في نومه وبين تقلبه وقبض يده وبسطها، كما نفرق بينهما في حق المستيقظ من غير فرق ، ومن رام التسوية بينهما في النائم لم يبعد عنه التشكيك في تسويتهما في حق اليقظان وهو بعيد عن المعقول قال : هذا وإن كان في
The fourth type - the tenth topic: The saying of the philosophers about sleep and power. He had no knowledge of them, for sleep does not oppose power despite being opposed to knowledge and other perceptions by agreement of the rational people. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: They are not within his power, for sleep opposes power just as it opposes knowledge and all perceptions. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr and many of our companions hesitated and said: There is no certainty that those actions are acquired (muktasaba) for the sleeper nor that they are necessary (daruriyya) for him; rather both are possible without preference. Al-Amidi said: We may claim necessity in knowing that they are within the power of the sleeper, insofar as we distinguish between the trembling of his hand in his sleep and his turning over, grasping, and extending his hand, just as we distinguish between them in the case of the awake person without any difference. Whoever aims to equate them in the sleeper is not far from casting doubt on equating them in the case of the awake person, and that is far from reason. He said: This, even if it is in ...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the core debate about actions during sleep. It opens by stating that sleep does not oppose power (qudra) as it does knowledge. Then it gives three positions: (1) Al-Asfahani (Abu Ishaq) holds that such actions are not within the sleeper's power because sleep opposes power. (2) Al-Qadi Abu Bakr (al-Baqillani) suspends judgment—no certainty whether they are acquired (muktasaba) or necessary (daruriyya). (3) Al-Amidi argues for their being within power, based on the clear distinction between involuntary movements (like trembling) and voluntary ones (like turning over). This distinction holds whether asleep or awake; denying it would lead to absurdity. The passage ends with the beginning of al-Iji's own remark.

Key Terms
قدرة
ضرورة
اكتساب
توقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All actions during sleep are beyond the sleeper's power because sleep renders him incapable.
Correction: Al-Amidi shows that we can distinguish between voluntary and involuntary movements even in sleep, indicating some actions are within the sleeper's power.

Checkpoint
Which scholar suspended judgment on whether a sleeper's actions are within his power?
A) Al-Asfahani
B) Al-Qadi Abu Bakr
C) Al-Amidi
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The debate over actions during sleep reveals fundamental disagreements about the nature of power and how to distinguish voluntary from involuntary acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely performed actions during sleep, like turning over or speaking. Based on this lesson, do you consider those actions within your power? How does that affect your sense of responsibility before Allah for them?
?
Al-Amidi argues that denying the distinction between voluntary and involuntary in sleep would lead to denying it in wakefulness. Examine your own life: Is there any area where you treat your actions as beyond your control to avoid accountability? How does this argument challenge that excuse?
?
The scholars here differ on whether sleep opposes power. Reflect on a time you were partially asleep or drowsy and made a decision. Did you feel you had full control? How does this debate inform your understanding of the conditions of moral agency?

Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's Defense of Dream Perceptions
Al-Ustadh's argument for dream reality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's argument that dreams are real perceptions, and how he resolves the apparent contradiction with sleep being the opposite of perception.

Core Idea
Dreams are not illusions; they are as real as waking perceptions.

Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq presents a bold claim: dreams are genuine perceptions, indistinguishable from waking experience. He argues that if we doubt dreams, we must doubt all perception, leading to sophistry. He also addresses the objection that sleep is contrary to perception by locating perception and sleep in different parts of the human being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the structure.
2
Understand al-Ustadh's main claim
Identify that he asserts dreams are 'idrak haqq' (real perception) without doubt.
The key term is إدراك حق.
3
Analyze his indistinguishability argument
He says there is no difference between dream perceptions and waking perceptions.
4
Consider the consequence of doubting
If we doubt dreams, we must doubt all, leading to sophistry.
Sophistry (السفسطة) means total skepticism.
5
Examine his resolution of the sleep-perception conflict
He says perception and sleep occur in different parts of the person, so they don't conflict.
This avoids the problem of opposites in one locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : إنه أي المنام إدراك حق بلا شبهة إذ لا فرق بين ما يجده النائم من نفسه في نومه من إبصار للمبصرات وسمع للمسموعات وذوق للمذوقات وغيرها من الإدراكات وبين ما يجده اليقظان في يقظته من إدراكاته فلو جاز التشكيك فيه أي فيما يجده النائم لجاز التشكيك فيما يجده اليقظان ولزم السفسطة والقدح في الأمور المعلومة حقيقتها بالبديهة ولم يخالف الأستاذ في كون النوم ضداً للإدراك لكنه زعم أن الإدراك يقوم بجزء من أجزاء الإنسان غير ما يقوم به النوم من أجزائه، فلا يلزم اجتماع الضدين في محل واحد وقال الحكماء : المدرك في النوم يوجد في الحس المشترك وذلك أن الحس المشترك مجمع المحسوسات الظاهرة، فإن الحواس الظاهرة إذا أخذت المحسوسات الخارجية وأدتها إلى الحس المشترك صارت تلك الصور مشاهدة ثم إن القوة المتخيلة التي من شأنها تركيب الصور إذا ركبت صورة فربما انطبعت تلك الصورة في الحس المشترك وصارت مشاهدة على حسب مشاهدة الصور الخارجية، فإن الخارجية لم تكن مشاهدة لكونها صورة الخارجية، بل لكونها مرتسمة في الحس المشترك
And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'It, i.e., the dream, is a real perception without doubt, for there is no difference between what the sleeper finds in himself in his sleep, of seeing visible things, hearing audible things, tasting tastes, and other perceptions, and between what the awake person finds in his wakefulness of his perceptions. So if it were permissible to doubt it, i.e., what the sleeper finds, it would be permissible to doubt what the awake person finds, and sophistry (safsata) would follow, and an attack on matters whose reality is known by intuition. And al-Ustadh did not disagree that sleep is contrary to perception, but he claimed that perception subsists in a part of the parts of the person other than the part in which sleep subsists, so the meeting of two contraries in one locus does not follow.' And the philosophers said: 'The perceived in sleep exists in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). That is because the common sense is the gathering place of the external sensibles. For the external senses, when they take external sensibles and convey them to the common sense, those images become witnessed. Then the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutakhayyila), whose characteristic is to combine images, when it combines an image, perhaps that image is imprinted in the common sense and becomes witnessed in the manner of witnessing external images. For the external images were not witnessed because they are images of externals, but because they are impressed in the common sense.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative view from al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and then the philosophers (al-hukama'). Al-Ustadh argues that dreams are real perceptions (idrak haqq) without any doubt. He bases this on the indistinguishability between dream perceptions and waking perceptions. If you doubt dream perceptions, you would have to doubt waking perceptions, leading to sophistry (safsata) and undermining self-evident truths. He acknowledges that sleep is contrary to perception, but he resolves this by stating that perception occurs in a different part of the person than the part affected by sleep, so there is no conflict of opposites in one locus. The philosophers then provide a physiological explanation: the perceived in sleep occurs in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), which is the center where external sensations converge. The external senses bring data to the common sense, and images are seen there. The imaginative faculty can also combine images and imprint them in the common sense, making them appear as witnessed, just like external images. Thus, dreams are real perceptions in the common sense, not false.

Key Terms
الأستاذ أبو إسحاق
إدراك حق
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ustadh denies that sleep is opposite to perception.
Correction: He acknowledges it but resolves it by different loci.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ustadh argue would follow if we were to doubt dream perceptions?
A) Only dreams would be doubted.
B) Waking perceptions would also be doubted, leading to sophistry.
C) Nothing would follow.
D) Dreams would be considered false.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ustadh defends the reality of dreams by arguing they are indistinguishable from waking perceptions, and any doubt would lead to total skepticism, which he avoids by locating perception and sleep in different parts of the person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your dreams are as real as waking experiences, then who is the real 'you'—the one dreaming or the one awake? How does this compel you to see yourself as more than just a physical body?
?
Are there beliefs about Allah or the unseen that you treat like 'dreams'—real but not fully trusted? What would it take for you to treat them as 'waking' certainties?
?
Al-Ustadh warns that doubting dreams leads to doubting everything. Is there any area of your faith where you have allowed a small doubt to grow into a pattern of skepticism? What will you do today to root out that doubt?
